<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Water</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Chemical compound with formula H‚ÇÇO</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"H2O" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/H%E2%82%82O_(disambiguation)" title="H‚ÇÇO (disambiguation)">H‚ÇÇO (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Water_(disambiguation)" title="Water (disambiguation)">Water (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti title="Cloud">Clouds</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a> condense from <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gaseous</a> <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>.</div></div></div></div></div>
<p><b>Water</b> (chemical formula <b>H</b><sub><b>2</b></sub><b>O</b>) is an <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_compound" title="Inorganic compound">inorganic</a>, transparent, tasteless, odorless, and <a href="/wiki/Color_of_water" title="Color of water">nearly colorless</a> <a href="/wiki/Chemical_substance" title="Chemical substance">chemical substance</a>, which is the main constituent of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Fluid" title="Fluid">fluids</a> of all known living organisms (in which it acts as a <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>). It is vital for all known forms of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a>, despite providing neither <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food, energy</a>, nor organic <a href="/wiki/Micronutrient" title="Micronutrient">micronutrients</a>. Its <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">chemical formula</a>, H<sub>2</sub>O, indicates that each of its <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> contains one <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and two <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>, connected by <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent bonds</a>. The hydrogen atoms are attached to the oxygen atom at an angle of 104.45¬∞.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> "Water" is also the name of the liquid state of H<sub>2</sub>O at <a href="/wiki/Standard_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard temperature and pressure">standard temperature and pressure</a>. 
</p><p>A number of natural states of water exist. It forms <a href="/wiki/Precipitation" title="Precipitation">precipitation</a> in the form of rain and <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosols</a> in the form of fog. <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">Clouds</a> consist of suspended droplets of water and <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a>, its solid state. When finely divided, <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystalline</a> ice may precipitate in the form of <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">snow</a>. The gaseous state of water is <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a> or <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a>.
</p><p>Water covers about 71% of the Earth's surface, mostly in seas and oceans (about 96.5%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSS_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSS-3">[3]</a></sup> Small portions of water occur as <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> (1.7%), in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciers" title="Glaciers">glaciers</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_caps" title="Ice caps">ice caps</a> of <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> (1.7%), and in the air as <a href="/wiki/Vapor" title="Vapor">vapor</a>, clouds (consisting of ice and liquid water suspended in air), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a> (0.001%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b1_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-b1-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Water moves continually through the <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a> of <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpiration</a> (<a href="/wiki/Evapotranspiration" title="Evapotranspiration">evapotranspiration</a>), <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condensation</a>, precipitation, and <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">runoff</a>, usually reaching the sea.
</p><p>Water plays an important role in the <a href="/wiki/World_economy" title="World economy">world economy</a>. Approximately 70% of the freshwater used by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humans" title="Humans">humans</a> <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_agriculture" title="Environmental impact of agriculture">goes to agriculture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baroni2007_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baroni2007-6">[6]</a></sup> Fishing in salt and fresh water bodies is a major source of food for many parts of the world, providing 6.5% of global protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Much of the long-distance trade of commodities (such as oil, natural gas, and manufactured products) is <a href="/wiki/Maritime_transport" title="Maritime transport">transported by boats</a> through seas, rivers, lakes, and canals. Large quantities of water, ice, and steam are used for cooling and heating, in industry and homes. Water is an excellent solvent for a wide variety of substances both mineral and organic; as such it is widely used in industrial processes, and in cooking and washing. Water, ice and snow are also central to many sports and other forms of entertainment, such as swimming, pleasure boating, boat racing, surfing, sport fishing, diving, ice skating and skiing.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The word <i>water</i> comes from <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <span title="Old English (ca. 450-1100)-language text"><i lang="ang">w√¶ter</i></span>, from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic</a> *<i>watar</i> (source also of <a href="/wiki/Old_Saxon" title="Old Saxon">Old Saxon</a> <span title="Old Saxon-language text"><i lang="osx">watar</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Frisian" title="Old Frisian">Old Frisian</a> <span title="Old Frisian-language text"><i lang="ofs">wetir</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a> <span title="Dutch-language text"><i lang="nl">water</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a> <span title="Old High German (ca. 750-1050)-language text"><i lang="goh">wazzar</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a> <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Wasser</i></span>, <span title="Old Norse-language text"><i lang="non">vatn</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Gothic_language" title="Gothic language">Gothic</a> <span title="Gothic-language text"><span lang="got">êçÖêå∞êçÑêçâ</span></span> (<span title="Gothic-language romanization"><i lang="got-Latn">wato</i></span>), from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> *<i>wod-or</i>, suffixed form of root *<i>wed-</i> ("water"; "wet").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Also <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognate</a>, through the Indo-European root, with <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <span title="Greek-language text"><span lang="el">œçŒ¥œâœÅ</span></span> (<span title="Greek-language romanization"><i lang="el-Latn">√Ωdor</i></span>), <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a> <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">–≤–æ–¥–∞ÃÅ</span></span> (<span title="Russian-language romanization"><i lang="ru-Latn">vod√°</i></span>), <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a> <span title="Irish-language text"><i lang="ga">uisce</i></span>, and <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a> <span title="Albanian-language text"><i lang="sq">uj√´</i></span>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Origin_of_water_on_Earth#History_of_water_on_Earth" title="Origin of water on Earth">Origin of water on Earth ¬ß¬†History of water on Earth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Properties_of_water#History" title="Properties of water">Properties of water ¬ß¬†History</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Properties_of_water" title="Properties of water">Properties of water</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Water_molecule_(1).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="350" decoding="async" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Water_molecule_%281%29.svg/220px-Water_molecule_%281%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Water_molecule_%281%29.svg/330px-Water_molecule_%281%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Water_molecule_%281%29.svg/440px-Water_molecule_%281%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Water_molecule_(1).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A water molecule consists of two hydrogen atoms and one oxygen atom</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Water_(data_page)" title="Water (data page)">Water (data page)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_model" title="Water model">Water model</a></div><p>Water (<span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">H</a><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span><a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">O</a></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_polarity" title="Chemical polarity">polar</a> <a href="/wiki/Inorganic_compound" title="Inorganic compound">inorganic compound</a>. At <a href="/wiki/Room_temperature" title="Room temperature">room temperature</a> it is a <a href="/wiki/Taste" title="Taste">tasteless</a> and <a href="/wiki/Odor" title="Odor">odorless</a> <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a>, nearly <a href="/wiki/Transparency_and_translucency" title="Transparency and translucency">colorless</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Color_of_water" title="Color of water">hint of blue</a>. This simplest <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chalcogenide" title="Hydrogen chalcogenide">hydrogen chalcogenide</a> is by far the most studied chemical compound and is described as the "universal solvent" for its ability to dissolve many substances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> This allows it to be the "<a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> of life":<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> indeed, water as found in nature almost always includes various dissolved substances, and special steps are required to obtain chemically <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pure_water" title="Pure water">pure water</a>. Water is the only common substance to exist as a <a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a>, liquid, and <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> in normal terrestrial conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="States">States</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:States_of_Matter.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="310" data-file-width="310" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/States_of_Matter.svg/220px-States_of_Matter.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/States_of_Matter.svg/330px-States_of_Matter.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/States_of_Matter.svg/440px-States_of_Matter.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:States_of_Matter.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The three common states of matter</div></div></div>
<p>Along with <i>oxidane</i>, <i>water</i> is one of the two official names for the chemical compound <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> it is also the liquid phase of <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The other two common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/States_of_matter" title="States of matter">states of matter</a> of water are the solid phase, <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a>, and the gaseous phase, <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a> or <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steam</a>. The addition or removal of heat can cause <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">phase transitions</a>: <a href="/wiki/Freezing" title="Freezing">freezing</a> (water to ice), <a href="/wiki/Melting" title="Melting">melting</a> (ice to water), <a href="/wiki/Vaporization" title="Vaporization">vaporization</a> (water to vapor), <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condensation</a> (vapor to water), <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">sublimation</a> (ice to vapor) and <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(phase_transition)" title="Deposition (phase transition)">deposition</a> (vapor to ice).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belnay_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belnay-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Density">Density</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Frost_weathering" title="Frost weathering">Frost weathering</a></div>
<p>Water differs from most liquids in that it becomes less <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">dense</a> as it freezes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> In 1¬†atm pressure, it reaches its maximum density of 1,000¬†kg/m<sup>3</sup> (62.43¬†lb/cu¬†ft) at 3.98¬†¬∞C (39.16¬†¬∞F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatureWaterStructure_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-NatureWaterStructure-18">[18]</a></sup> The density of ice is 917¬†kg/m<sup>3</sup> (57.25¬†lb/cu¬†ft), an expansion of 9%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> This expansion can exert enormous pressure, bursting pipes and cracking rocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MM_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-MM-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a lake or ocean, water at 4¬†¬∞C (39.2¬†¬∞F) sinks to the bottom, and ice forms on the surface, floating on the liquid water. This ice insulates the water below, preventing it from freezing solid. Without this protection, most aquatic organisms residing in lakes would perish during the winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetism">Magnetism</span></h4>
<p>Water is a <a href="/wiki/Diamagnetism" title="Diamagnetism">diamagnetic</a> material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-23">[23]</a></sup> Though interaction is weak, with superconducting magnets it can attain a notable interaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Phase_transitions">Phase transitions</span></h4>
<p>At a pressure of one <a href="/wiki/Standard_atmosphere_(unit)" title="Standard atmosphere (unit)">atmosphere</a> (atm), ice melts or water freezes at 0¬†¬∞C (32¬†¬∞F) and water boils or vapor condenses at 100¬†¬∞C (212¬†¬∞F). However, even below the boiling point, water can change to vapor at its surface by <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> (vaporization throughout the liquid is known as <a href="/wiki/Boiling" title="Boiling">boiling</a>). Sublimation and deposition also occur on surfaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belnay_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Belnay-15">[15]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Frost" title="Frost">frost</a> is deposited on cold surfaces while <a href="/wiki/Snowflake" title="Snowflake">snowflakes</a> form by deposition on an aerosol particle or ice nucleus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> In the process of <a href="/wiki/Freeze-drying" title="Freeze-drying">freeze-drying</a>, a food is frozen and then stored at low pressure so the ice on its surface sublimates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FreezeDrying_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FreezeDrying-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>The melting and boiling points depend on pressure. A good approximation for the rate of change of the melting temperature with pressure is given by the <a href="/wiki/Clausius%E2%80%93Clapeyron_relation" title="Clausius‚ÄìClapeyron relation">Clausius‚ÄìClapeyron relation</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {dT}{dP}}={\frac {T\left(v_{\text{L}}-v_{\text{S}}\right)}{L_{\text{f}}}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle L_{\text{f}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle L_{\text{f}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/064c791dfa8796c6e27c056bf8a2103836de1ebd" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.427ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> is the molar <a href="/wiki/Latent_heat" title="Latent heat">latent heat</a> of melting. In most substances, the volume increases when melting occurs, so the melting temperature increases with pressure. However, because ice is less dense than water, the melting temperature decreases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oliveira_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oliveira-16">[16]</a></sup> In glaciers, <a href="/wiki/Pressure_melting_point" title="Pressure melting point">pressure melting</a> can occur under sufficiently thick volumes of ice, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Subglacial_lake" title="Subglacial lake">subglacial lakes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Clausius-Clapeyron relation also applies to the boiling point, but with the liquid/gas transition the vapor phase has a much lower density than the liquid phase, so the boiling point increases with pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Water can remain in a liquid state at high temperatures in the deep ocean or underground. For example, temperatures exceed 205¬†¬∞C (401¬†¬∞F) in <a href="/wiki/Old_Faithful" title="Old Faithful">Old Faithful</a>, a geyser in <a href="/wiki/Yellowstone_National_Park" title="Yellowstone National Park">Yellowstone National Park</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent" title="Hydrothermal vent">hydrothermal vents</a>, the temperature can exceed 400¬†¬∞C (752¬†¬∞F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>At <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a>, the boiling point of water is 100¬†¬∞C (212¬†¬∞F). As atmospheric pressure decreases with altitude, the boiling point decreases by 1¬†¬∞C every 274¬†meters. <a href="/wiki/High-altitude_cooking" title="High-altitude cooking">High-altitude cooking</a> takes longer than sea-level cooking. For example, at 1,524 metres (5,000¬†ft), cooking time must be increased by a fourth to achieve the desired result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> (Conversely, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressure_cooker" title="Pressure cooker">pressure cooker</a> can be used to decrease cooking times by raising the boiling temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>)
In a vacuum, water will boil at room temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Triple_and_critical_points">Triple and critical points</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phase_diagram_of_water_simplified.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="379" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Phase_diagram_of_water_simplified.svg/220px-Phase_diagram_of_water_simplified.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Phase_diagram_of_water_simplified.svg/330px-Phase_diagram_of_water_simplified.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Phase_diagram_of_water_simplified.svg/440px-Phase_diagram_of_water_simplified.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phase_diagram_of_water_simplified.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Phase diagram of water (simplified)</div></div></div>
<p>On a pressure/temperature <a href="/wiki/Phase_diagram" title="Phase diagram">phase diagram</a> (see figure), there are curves separating solid from vapor, vapor from liquid, and liquid from solid. These meet at a single point called the <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a>, where all three phases can coexist. The triple point is at a temperature of 273.16¬†K (0.01¬†¬∞C) and a pressure of 611.657 pascals (0.00604¬†atm);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> it is the lowest pressure at which liquid water can exist. <a href="/wiki/2019_redefinition_of_the_SI_base_units" title="2019 redefinition of the SI base units">Until 2019</a>, the triple point was used to define the <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">Kelvin temperature scale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brochure9_2019_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brochure9_2019-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>The water/vapor phase curve terminates at 647.096¬†K (373.946¬†¬∞C; 705.103¬†¬∞F) and 22.064 megapascals (3,200.1¬†psi; 217.75¬†atm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAPWS95_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAPWS95-37">[37]</a></sup> This is known as the <a href="/wiki/Critical_point_(thermodynamics)" title="Critical point (thermodynamics)">critical point</a>. At higher temperatures and pressures the liquid and vapor phases form a continuous phase called a <a href="/wiki/Supercritical_fluid" title="Supercritical fluid">supercritical fluid</a>. It can be gradually compressed or expanded between gas-like and liquid-like densities, its properties (which are quite different from those of ambient water) are sensitive to density. For example, for suitable pressures and temperatures it can <a href="/wiki/Miscibility" title="Miscibility">mix freely</a> with <a href="/wiki/Chemical_polarity#Nonpolar_molecules" title="Chemical polarity">nonpolar compounds</a>, including most <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a>. This makes it useful in a variety of applications including high-temperature <a href="/wiki/Electrochemistry" title="Electrochemistry">electrochemistry</a> and as an ecologically benign solvent or <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyst</a> in chemical reactions involving organic compounds. In Earth's mantle, it acts as a solvent during mineral formation, dissolution and deposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Phases_of_ice_and_water">Phases of ice and water</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">Ice</a></div>
<p>The normal form of ice on the surface of Earth is <a href="/wiki/Ice_Ih" title="Ice Ih">Ice Ih</a>, a phase that forms crystals with <a href="/wiki/Hexagonal_crystal_family" title="Hexagonal crystal family">hexagonal symmetry</a>. Another with <a href="/wiki/Cubic_crystal_system" title="Cubic crystal system">cubic crystalline symmetry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ice_Ic" title="Ice Ic">Ice Ic</a>, can occur in the upper atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> As the pressure increases, ice forms other <a href="/wiki/Crystal_structure" title="Crystal structure">crystal structures</a>. As of 2019, 17 have been experimentally confirmed and several more are predicted theoretically (see <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">Ice</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The 18th form of ice, ice XVIII, a face-centred-cubic, superionic ice phase, was discovered when a droplet of water was subject to a shock wave that raised the water's pressure to millions of atmospheres and its temperature to thousands of degrees, resulting in a structure of rigid oxygen atoms in which hydrogen atoms flowed freely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sokol2021_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sokol2021-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Millotetal2019_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Millotetal2019-43">[43]</a></sup> When sandwiched between layers of <a href="/wiki/Graphene" title="Graphene">graphene</a>, ice forms a square lattice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The details of the chemical nature of liquid water are not well understood; some theories suggest that its unusual behaviour is due to the existence of 2 liquid states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NatureWaterStructure_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-NatureWaterStructure-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taste_and_odor">Taste and odor</span></h3>
<p>Pure water is usually described as tasteless and odorless, although <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humans" title="Humans">humans</a> have specific sensors that can feel the presence of water in their mouths,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid28553944_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid28553944-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emo_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-emo-49">[49]</a></sup> and frogs are known to be able to smell it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frog_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-frog-50">[50]</a></sup> However, water from ordinary sources (including bottled mineral water) usually has many dissolved substances, that may give it varying tastes and odors. Humans and other animals have developed senses that enable them to evaluate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potability" title="Potability">potability</a> of water in order to avoid water that is too salty or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Putrid" title="Putrid">putrid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-candau_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-candau-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Color_and_appearance">Color and appearance</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Color_of_water" title="Color of water">Color of water</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_absorption_by_water" title="Electromagnetic absorption by water">Electromagnetic absorption by water</a></div>
<p>Pure water is <a href="/wiki/Visual_perception" title="Visual perception">visibly</a> blue due to <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_absorption_by_water" title="Electromagnetic absorption by water">absorption</a> of light in the region ca. 600¬†nm ‚Äì 800¬†nm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The color can be easily observed in a glass of tap-water placed against a pure white background, in daylight. The principal absorption bands responsible for the color are <a href="/wiki/Overtone_band" title="Overtone band">overtones</a> of the O‚ÄìH stretching <a href="/wiki/Molecular_vibration" title="Molecular vibration">vibrations</a>. The apparent intensity of the color increases with the depth of the water column, following <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beer%27s_law" title="Beer's law">Beer's law</a>. This also applies, for example, with a swimming pool when the light source is sunlight reflected from the pool's white tiles.
</p><p>In nature, the color may also be modified from blue to green due to the presence of suspended solids or algae.
</p><p>In industry, <a href="/wiki/Near-infrared_spectroscopy" title="Near-infrared spectroscopy">near-infrared spectroscopy</a> is used with aqueous solutions as the greater intensity of the lower overtones of water means that glass <a href="/wiki/Cuvette" title="Cuvette">cuvettes</a> with short path-length may be employed. To observe the fundamental stretching absorption spectrum of water or of an aqueous solution in the region around 3,500¬†cm<sup>‚àí1</sup> (2.85¬†Œºm)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> a path length of about 25¬†Œºm is needed. Also, the cuvette must be both transparent around 3500¬†cm<sup>‚àí1</sup> and insoluble in water; <a href="/wiki/Calcium_fluoride" title="Calcium fluoride">calcium fluoride</a> is one material that is in common use for the cuvette windows with aqueous solutions.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Raman_spectroscopy" title="Raman spectroscopy">Raman-active</a> fundamental vibrations may be observed with, for example, a 1¬†cm sample cell.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aquatic_plant" title="Aquatic plant">Aquatic plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthetic</a> organisms can live in water up to hundreds of meters deep, because <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a> can reach them.
Practically no sunlight reaches the parts of the oceans below 1,000 meters (3,300¬†ft) of depth.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> of liquid water (1.333 at 20¬†¬∞C (68¬†¬∞F)) is much higher than that of air (1.0), similar to those of <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkanes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>, but lower than those of <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a> (1.473), <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a> (1.501), <a href="/wiki/Carbon_disulfide" title="Carbon disulfide">carbon disulfide</a> (1.627), and common types of glass (1.4 to 1.6). The refraction index of ice (1.31) is lower than that of liquid water.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polar_molecule">Polar molecule</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tetrahedral_Structure_of_Water.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="448" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Tetrahedral_Structure_of_Water.png/220px-Tetrahedral_Structure_of_Water.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Tetrahedral_Structure_of_Water.png/330px-Tetrahedral_Structure_of_Water.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Tetrahedral_Structure_of_Water.png/440px-Tetrahedral_Structure_of_Water.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tetrahedral_Structure_of_Water.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tetrahedral structure of water</div></div></div>
<p>In a water molecule, the hydrogen atoms form a 104.5¬∞ angle with the oxygen atom. The hydrogen atoms are close to two corners of a tetrahedron centered on the oxygen. At the other two corners are <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lone_pairs" title="Lone pairs">lone pairs</a></i> of valence electrons that do not participate in the bonding. In a perfect tetrahedron, the atoms would form a 109.5¬∞ angle, but the repulsion between the lone pairs is greater than the repulsion between the hydrogen atoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The O‚ÄìH bond length is about 0.096¬†nm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other substances have a tetrahedral molecular structure, for example, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">CH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> (<span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>S</span>). However, oxygen is more <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegative</a> (holds on to its electrons more tightly) than most other elements, so the oxygen atom retains a negative charge while the hydrogen atoms are positively charged. Along with the bent structure, this gives the molecule an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_dipole_moment" title="Electrical dipole moment">electrical dipole moment</a> and it is classified as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polar_molecule" title="Polar molecule">polar molecule</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Water is a good polar <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a>, that dissolves many <a href="/wiki/Salt_(chemistry)" title="Salt (chemistry)">salts</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophilic" title="Hydrophilic">hydrophilic</a> organic molecules such as sugars and simple alcohols such as <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a>. Water also dissolves many gases, such as oxygen and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>‚Äîthe latter giving the fizz of <a href="/wiki/Carbonation" title="Carbonation">carbonated</a> beverages, <a href="/wiki/Sparkling_wine" title="Sparkling wine">sparkling wines</a> and beers. In addition, many substances in living organisms, such as <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polysaccharide" title="Polysaccharide">polysaccharides</a>, are dissolved in water. The interactions between water and the subunits of these biomacromolecules shape <a href="/wiki/Protein_folding" title="Protein folding">protein folding</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Base_pairing" title="Base pairing">DNA base pairing</a>, and other phenomena crucial to life (<a href="/wiki/Hydrophobic_effect" title="Hydrophobic effect">hydrophobic effect</a>).
</p><p>Many organic substances (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipids" title="Lipids">fats and oils</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkanes" title="Alkanes">alkanes</a>) are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrophobic" title="Hydrophobic">hydrophobic</a>, that is, insoluble in water. Many inorganic substances are insoluble too, including most metal <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfide" title="Sulfide">sulfides</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicates</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen_bonding">Hydrogen bonding</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bonding_of_water" title="Chemical bonding of water">Chemical bonding of water</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="397" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg/220px-3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg/330px-3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg/440px-3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:3D_model_hydrogen_bonds_in_water.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Model of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bond" title="Hydrogen bond">hydrogen bonds</a> (1) between molecules of water</div></div></div>
<p>Because of its polarity, a molecule of water in the liquid or solid state can form up to four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bonds" title="Hydrogen bonds">hydrogen bonds</a> with neighboring molecules. Hydrogen bonds are about ten times as strong as the <a href="/wiki/Van_der_Waals_force" title="Van der Waals force">Van der Waals force</a> that attracts molecules to each other in most liquids. This is the reason why the melting and boiling points of water are much higher than those of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chalcogenide" title="Hydrogen chalcogenide">other analogous compounds</a> like hydrogen sulfide. They also explain its exceptionally high <a href="/wiki/Specific_heat_capacity" title="Specific heat capacity">specific heat capacity</a> (about 4.2 <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">J</a>/g/K), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_of_fusion" title="Heat of fusion">heat of fusion</a> (about 333 J/g), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_of_vaporization" title="Heat of vaporization">heat of vaporization</a> (<span class="nowrap">2257 J/g</span>), and <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a> (between 0.561 and 0.679 W/m/K). These properties make water more effective at moderating Earth's <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a>, by storing heat and transporting it between the oceans and the atmosphere. The hydrogen bonds of water are around 23 kJ/mol (compared to a covalent O-H bond at 492 kJ/mol). Of this, it is estimated that 90% is attributable to electrostatics, while the remaining 10% is partially covalent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>These bonds are the cause of water's high <a href="/wiki/Surface_tension" title="Surface tension">surface tension</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> and capillary forces. The <a href="/wiki/Capillary_action" title="Capillary action">capillary action</a> refers to the tendency of water to move up a narrow tube against the force of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>. This property is relied upon by all <a href="/wiki/Vascular_plant" title="Vascular plant">vascular plants</a>, such as trees.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:316px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heat_capacity_of_water_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="696" data-file-width="1060" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Heat_capacity_of_water_2.jpg/314px-Heat_capacity_of_water_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Heat_capacity_of_water_2.jpg/471px-Heat_capacity_of_water_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Heat_capacity_of_water_2.jpg/628px-Heat_capacity_of_water_2.jpg 2x" width="314"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heat_capacity_of_water_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Specific heat capacity of water<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-ionization">Self-ionization</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Self-ionization_of_water" title="Self-ionization of water">Self-ionization of water</a></div>
<p>Water is a weak solution of hydronium hydroxide ‚Äì there is an equilibrium <span class="chemf nowrap">2H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> ‚áî <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">OH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">‚àí</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, in combination with solvation of the resulting <a href="/wiki/Hydronium" title="Hydronium">hydronium</a> ions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electrical_conductivity_and_electrolysis">Electrical conductivity and electrolysis</span></h3>
<p>Pure water has a low <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a>, which increases with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dissolution_(chemistry)" title="Dissolution (chemistry)">dissolution</a> of a small amount of ionic material such as <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">common salt</a>.
</p><p>Liquid water can be split into the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a> hydrogen and oxygen by passing an electric current through it‚Äîa process called <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis_of_water" title="Electrolysis of water">electrolysis</a>. The decomposition requires more energy input than the <a href="/wiki/Standard_enthalpy_of_formation" title="Standard enthalpy of formation">heat released by the inverse process</a> (285.8 kJ/<a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mol</a>, or 15.9 MJ/kg).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical_properties">Mechanical properties</span></h3>
<p>Liquid water can be assumed to be incompressible for most purposes: its compressibility ranges from 4.4 to <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6990510000000000000‚ô†"></span>5.1<span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">√ó</span>10<sup>‚àí10</sup>¬†Pa<sup>‚àí1</sup></span> in ordinary conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Even in oceans at 4¬†km depth, where the pressure is 400 atm, water suffers only a 1.8% decrease in volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nave_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-nave-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a> of water is about 10<sup>‚àí3</sup> Pa¬∑<a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">s</a> or 0.01 <a href="/wiki/Poise_(unit)" title="Poise (unit)">poise</a> at 20¬†¬∞C (68¬†¬∞F), and the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">speed of sound</a> in liquid water ranges between 1,400 and 1,540 meters per second (4,600 and 5,100¬†ft/s) depending on temperature. Sound travels long distances in water with little attenuation, especially at low frequencies (roughly 0.03 <a href="/wiki/Decibel" title="Decibel">dB</a>/km for 1 k<a href="/wiki/Hertz" title="Hertz">Hz</a>), a property that is exploited by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetaceans" title="Cetaceans">cetaceans</a> and humans for communication and environment sensing (<a href="/wiki/Sonar" title="Sonar">sonar</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPLcalc_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-NPLcalc-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactivity">Reactivity</span></h3>
<p>Metallic elements which are more <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electropositive</a> than hydrogen, particularly the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkali_metals" title="Alkali metals">alkali metals</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metals" title="Alkaline earth metals">alkaline earth metals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">cesium</a> displace hydrogen from water, forming <a href="/wiki/Hydroxide" title="Hydroxide">hydroxides</a> and releasing hydrogen. At high temperatures, carbon reacts with steam to form <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> and hydrogen.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="On_Earth">On Earth</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hydrology" title="Hydrology">Hydrology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_distribution_on_Earth" title="Water distribution on Earth">Water distribution on Earth</a></div>
<p>Hydrology is the study of the movement, distribution, and quality of water throughout the Earth. The study of the distribution of water is <a href="/wiki/Hydrography" title="Hydrography">hydrography</a>. The study of the distribution and movement of <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> is <a href="/wiki/Hydrogeology" title="Hydrogeology">hydrogeology</a>, of glaciers is <a href="/wiki/Glaciology" title="Glaciology">glaciology</a>, of inland waters is <a href="/wiki/Limnology" title="Limnology">limnology</a> and distribution of oceans is <a href="/wiki/Oceanography" title="Oceanography">oceanography</a>. Ecological processes with hydrology are in the focus of <a href="/wiki/Ecohydrology" title="Ecohydrology">ecohydrology</a>.
</p><p>The collective mass of water found on, under, and over the surface of a planet is called the <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a>. Earth's approximate water volume (the total water supply of the world) is 1.386 √ó 10<sup>9</sup> cubic kilometers (3.33 √ó 10<sup>8</sup> cubic miles).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b1_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-b1-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Liquid water is found in <a href="/wiki/Body_of_water" title="Body of water">bodies of water</a>, such as an ocean, sea, lake, river, stream, <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canal</a>, pond, or <a href="/wiki/Puddle" title="Puddle">puddle</a>. The majority of water on Earth is <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a>. Water is also present in the atmosphere in solid, liquid, and vapor states. It also exists as groundwater in <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a>.
</p><p>Water is important in many geological processes. Groundwater is present in most <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocks</a>, and the pressure of this groundwater affects patterns of <a href="/wiki/Fault_(geology)" title="Fault (geology)">faulting</a>. Water in the <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantle</a> is responsible for the melt that produces <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subduction_zone" title="Subduction zone">subduction zones</a>. On the surface of the Earth, water is important in both chemical and physical <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a> processes. Water, and to a lesser but still significant extent, ice, are also responsible for a large amount of <a href="/wiki/Sediment_transport" title="Sediment transport">sediment transport</a> that occurs on the surface of the earth. <a href="/wiki/Deposition_(geology)" title="Deposition (geology)">Deposition</a> of transported sediment forms many types of <a href="/wiki/Sedimentary_rock" title="Sedimentary rock">sedimentary rocks</a>, which make up the <a href="/wiki/Geologic_record" title="Geologic record">geologic record</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Earth" title="History of the Earth">Earth history</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_cycle">Water cycle</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">Water cycle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Water_cycle.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="589" data-file-width="860" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Water_cycle.png/220px-Water_cycle.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Water_cycle.png/330px-Water_cycle.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Water_cycle.png/440px-Water_cycle.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Water_cycle.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">Water cycle</a></div></div></div>
<p>The water cycle (known scientifically as the hydrologic cycle) refers to the continuous exchange of water within the <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a>, between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_atmosphere" title="Earth atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> water, <a href="/wiki/Surface_water" title="Surface water">surface water</a>, groundwater, and plants.
</p><p>Water moves perpetually through each of these regions in the <i>water cycle</i> consisting of the following transfer processes:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> from oceans and other water bodies into the air and <a href="/wiki/Transpiration" title="Transpiration">transpiration</a> from land plants and animals into the air.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">precipitation</a>, from water vapor condensing from the air and falling to the earth or ocean.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Runoff_(water)" title="Runoff (water)">runoff</a> from the land usually reaching the sea.</li></ul>
<p>Most water vapors found mostly in the ocean returns to it, but winds carry water vapor over land at the same rate as runoff into the sea, about 47¬†<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_tonne_unit" title="Metric tonne unit">Tt</a> per year whilst evaporation and transpiration happening in land masses also contribute another 72¬†Tt per year. Precipitation, at a rate of 119 Tt per year over land, has several forms: most commonly rain, snow, and <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">hail</a>, with some contribution from <a href="/wiki/Fog" title="Fog">fog</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dew" title="Dew">dew</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Dew is small drops of water that are condensed when a high density of water vapor meets a cool surface. Dew usually forms in the morning when the temperature is the lowest, just before sunrise and when the temperature of the earth's surface starts to increase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Condensed water in the air may also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refract" title="Refract">refract</a> <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a> to produce <a href="/wiki/Rainbow" title="Rainbow">rainbows</a>.
</p><p>Water runoff often collects over <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">watersheds</a> flowing into rivers. Through <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>, runoff shapes the environment creating river <a href="/wiki/Valley" title="Valley">valleys</a> and <a href="/wiki/River_delta" title="River delta">deltas</a> which provide rich soil and level ground for the establishment of population centers. A flood occurs when an area of land, usually low-lying, is covered with water which occurs when a river overflows its banks or a storm surge happens. On the other hand, drought is an extended period of months or years when a region notes a deficiency in its water supply. This occurs when a region receives consistently below average precipitation either due to its topography or due to its location in terms of <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_resources">Water resources</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_resources" title="Water resources">Water resources</a></div>
<p>Water resources are <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">natural resources</a> of water that are potentially useful for humans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> for example as a source of drinking <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">water supply</a> or <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> water. Water occurs as both "stocks" and "flows". Water can be stored as lakes, water vapor, groundwater or aquifers, and ice and snow. Of the total volume of global freshwater, an estimated 69 percent is stored in glaciers and permanent snow cover; 30 percent is in groundwater; and the remaining 1 percent in lakes, rivers, the atmosphere, and biota.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The length of time water remains in storage is highly variable: some aquifers consist of water stored over thousands of years but lake volumes may fluctuate on a seasonal basis, decreasing during dry periods and increasing during wet ones. A substantial fraction of the water supply for some regions consists of water extracted from water stored in stocks, and when withdrawals exceed recharge, stocks decrease. By some estimates, as much as 30 percent of total water used for irrigation comes from unsustainable withdrawals of groundwater, causing <a href="/wiki/Overdrafting" title="Overdrafting">groundwater depletion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seawater_and_tides">Seawater and tides</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">Seawater</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tides" title="Tides">Tides</a></div>
<p>Seawater contains about 3.5% <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> on average, plus smaller amounts of other substances. The physical properties of seawater differ from <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a> in some important respects. It freezes at a lower temperature (about ‚àí1.9¬†¬∞C (28.6¬†¬∞F)) and its density increases with decreasing temperature to the freezing point, instead of reaching maximum density at a temperature above freezing. The salinity of water in major seas varies from about 0.7% in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> to 4.0% in the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>. (The <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea" title="Dead Sea">Dead Sea</a>, known for its ultra-high salinity levels of between 30 and 40%, is really a <a href="/wiki/Salt_lake" title="Salt lake">salt lake</a>.)
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">Tides</a> are the cyclic rising and falling of local sea levels caused by the <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidal forces</a> of the Moon and the Sun acting on the oceans. Tides cause changes in the depth of the marine and <a href="/wiki/Estuary" title="Estuary">estuarine</a> water bodies and produce oscillating currents known as tidal streams. The changing tide produced at a given location is the result of the changing positions of the Moon and Sun relative to the Earth coupled with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" title="Coriolis effect">effects of Earth rotation</a> and the local <a href="/wiki/Bathymetry" title="Bathymetry">bathymetry</a>. The strip of seashore that is submerged at high tide and exposed at low tide, the <a href="/wiki/Intertidal_zone" title="Intertidal zone">intertidal zone</a>, is an important ecological product of ocean tides.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1021810730">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery{width:100%!important}}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery{display:table}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-default{background:transparent;margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-center{margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-left{float:left}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-right{float:right}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-none{float:none}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery-collapsible{width:100%}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .title,.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .main,.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .footer{display:table-row}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .title>div{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0 0.6em 1.6em;text-align:center;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .main>div{display:table-cell}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .gallery{line-height:1.35em}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .footer>div{display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0 0.6em 1.6em;text-align:right;font-size:80%;line-height:1em}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .title>div *,.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .footer>div *{overflow:visible}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .gallerybox img{background:none!important}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .bordered-images .thumb img{border:solid #eaecf0 1px}.mw-parser-output .mod-gallery .whitebg .thumb{background:#fff!important}</style><div class="mod-gallery mod-gallery-default mod-gallery-center"><div class="title"><div>The <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Fundy" title="Bay of Fundy">Bay of Fundy</a> at high tide and low tide</div></div><div class="main"><div><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional nochecker bordered-images whitebg">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_life">Effects on life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Auto-and_heterotrophs.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1900" data-file-width="1633" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Auto-and_heterotrophs.svg/170px-Auto-and_heterotrophs.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Auto-and_heterotrophs.svg/255px-Auto-and_heterotrophs.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Auto-and_heterotrophs.svg/340px-Auto-and_heterotrophs.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Auto-and_heterotrophs.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Overview of <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> <span style="color:green;">(green)</span> and <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">respiration</a> <span style="color:red;">(red)</span></div></div></div>
<p>From a <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biological</a> standpoint, water has many distinct properties that are critical for the proliferation of life. It carries out this role by allowing <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic compounds</a> to react in ways that ultimately allow <a href="/wiki/Self-replication" title="Self-replication">replication</a>. All known forms of life depend on water. Water is vital both as a <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> in which many of the body's solutes dissolve and as an essential part of many <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolic</a> processes within the body. Metabolism is the sum total of <a href="/wiki/Anabolism" title="Anabolism">anabolism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Catabolism" title="Catabolism">catabolism</a>. In anabolism, water is removed from molecules (through energy requiring enzymatic chemical reactions) in order to grow larger molecules (e.g., starches, triglycerides, and proteins for storage of fuels and information). In catabolism, water is used to break bonds in order to generate smaller molecules (e.g., glucose, fatty acids, and amino acids to be used for fuels for energy use or other purposes). Without water, these particular metabolic processes could not exist.
</p><p>Water is fundamental to photosynthesis and respiration. Photosynthetic cells use the sun's energy to split off water's hydrogen from oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Hydrogen is combined with CO<sub>2</sub> (absorbed from air or water) to form glucose and release oxygen.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> All living cells use such fuels and oxidize the hydrogen and carbon to capture the sun's energy and reform water and CO<sub>2</sub> in the process (cellular respiration).
</p><p>Water is also central to acid-base neutrality and enzyme function. An acid, a hydrogen ion (H<sup>+</sup>, that is, a proton) donor, can be neutralized by a base, a proton acceptor such as a hydroxide ion (OH<sup>‚àí</sup>) to form water. Water is considered to be neutral, with a <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> (the negative log of the hydrogen ion concentration) of 7. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acids" title="Acids">Acids</a> have pH values less than 7 while <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">bases</a> have values greater than 7.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aquatic_life_forms">Aquatic life forms</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Hydrobiology" title="Hydrobiology">Hydrobiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marine_life" title="Marine life">Marine life</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_plant" title="Aquatic plant">Aquatic plant</a></div>
<p>Earth surface waters are filled with life. The earliest life forms appeared in water; nearly all fish live exclusively in water, and there are many types of marine mammals, such as dolphins and whales. Some kinds of animals, such as <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, spend portions of their lives in water and portions on land. Plants such as <a href="/wiki/Kelp" title="Kelp">kelp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> grow in the water and are the basis for some underwater ecosystems. <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">Plankton</a> is generally the foundation of the ocean <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a>.
</p><p>Aquatic vertebrates must obtain oxygen to survive, and they do so in various ways. Fish have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gills" title="Gills">gills</a> instead of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lungs" title="Lungs">lungs</a>, although some species of fish, such as the <a href="/wiki/Lungfish" title="Lungfish">lungfish</a>, have both. <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal" title="Marine mammal">Marine mammals</a>, such as dolphins, whales, <a href="/wiki/Otter" title="Otter">otters</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seals</a> need to surface periodically to breathe air. Some amphibians are able to absorb oxygen through their skin. Invertebrates exhibit a wide range of modifications to survive in poorly oxygenated waters including breathing tubes (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siphon_(insect)" title="Siphon (insect)">insect</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siphon_(mollusc)" title="Siphon (mollusc)">mollusc siphons</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gills" title="Gills">gills</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Carcinus" title="Carcinus">Carcinus</a></i>). However, as invertebrate life evolved in an aquatic habitat most have little or no specialization for respiration in water.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1021810730" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="mod-gallery mod-gallery-default mod-gallery-center"><div class="main"><div><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional nochecker bordered-images whitebg">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 215px"><div style="width: 215px">

</p>

</ul></div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_human_civilization">Effects on human civilization</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px 0;overflow:hidden;width:238px;border-collapse:collapse;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em}.mw-parser-output .ambox-speedy{border-left:10px solid #b32424;background-color:#fee7e6}.mw-parser-output .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png"
