<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Longwood_Gardens-Italian_Garden.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Longwood_Gardens-Italian_Garden.jpg/220px-Longwood_Gardens-Italian_Garden.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Longwood_Gardens-Italian_Garden.jpg/330px-Longwood_Gardens-Italian_Garden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Longwood_Gardens-Italian_Garden.jpg/440px-Longwood_Gardens-Italian_Garden.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Longwood_Gardens-Italian_Garden.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Water <a href="/wiki/Fountain" title="Fountain">fountain</a></div></div></div>
<p>Civilization has historically flourished around rivers and major waterways; <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, the so-called cradle of civilization, was situated between the major rivers <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>; the ancient society of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a> depended entirely upon the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a>. The early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilization" title="Indus Valley civilization">Indus Valley civilization</a> (c. 3300 BCE to 1300 BCE) developed along the Indus River and tributaries that flowed out of the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a>. <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> was also founded on the banks of the Italian river <a href="/wiki/Tiber" title="Tiber">Tiber</a>. Large <a href="/wiki/Metropolis" title="Metropolis">metropolises</a> like <a href="/wiki/Rotterdam" title="Rotterdam">Rotterdam</a>, <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> owe their success in part to their easy accessibility via water and the resultant expansion of trade. Islands with safe water ports, like <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, have flourished for the same reason. In places such as North Africa and the Middle East, where water is more scarce, access to clean drinking water was and is a major factor in human development.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_and_pollution">Health and pollution</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Field_Trip-_water_sampling.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Field_Trip-_water_sampling.jpg/220px-Field_Trip-_water_sampling.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Field_Trip-_water_sampling.jpg/330px-Field_Trip-_water_sampling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Field_Trip-_water_sampling.jpg/440px-Field_Trip-_water_sampling.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Field_Trip-_water_sampling.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An environmental science program ‚Äì a student from <a href="/wiki/Iowa_State_University" title="Iowa State University">Iowa State University</a> sampling water</div></div></div>
<p>Water fit for human consumption is called <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">drinking water</a> or potable water. Water that is not potable may be made potable by filtration or <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distillation</a>, or by a range of <a href="/wiki/Water_treatment" title="Water treatment">other methods</a>. More than 660 million people do not have access to safe drinking water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Water that is not fit for drinking but is not harmful to humans when used for swimming or bathing is called by various names other than potable or drinking water, and is sometimes called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safe_water" title="Safe water">safe water</a>, or "safe for bathing". Chlorine is a skin and mucous membrane irritant that is used to make water safe for bathing or drinking. Its use is highly technical and is usually monitored by government regulations (typically 1 part per million (ppm) for drinking water, and 1‚Äì2 ppm of chlorine not yet reacted with impurities for bathing water). Water for bathing may be maintained in satisfactory microbiological condition using chemical disinfectants such as <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> or by the use of <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> light.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Reclaimed_water" title="Reclaimed water">Water reclamation</a> is the process of converting wastewater (most commonly <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage</a>, also called municipal wastewater) into water that can be <a href="/wiki/Reuse" title="Reuse">reused</a> for other purposes. There are 2.3 billion people who reside in nations with water scarcities, which means that each individual receives less than 1 700 m3 of water annually. 380 billion m3 of municipal wastewater are produced globally each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:68_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-:68-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Freshwater is a renewable resource, recirculated by the natural <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrologic_cycle" title="Hydrologic cycle">hydrologic cycle</a>, but pressures over access to it result from the naturally uneven distribution in space and time, growing economic demands by agriculture and industry, and rising populations. Currently, nearly a billion people around the world lack access to safe, affordable water. In 2000, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> established the <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Development_Goals" title="Millennium Development Goals">Millennium Development Goals</a> for water to halve by 2015 the proportion of people worldwide without access to safe water and <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a>. Progress toward that goal was uneven, and in 2015 the UN committed to the <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goals" title="Sustainable Development Goals">Sustainable Development Goals</a> of achieving universal access to safe and affordable water and sanitation by 2030. Poor <a href="/wiki/Water_quality" title="Water quality">water quality</a> and bad sanitation are deadly; some five million deaths a year are caused by water-related diseases. The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> estimates that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Safe_water" title="Safe water">safe water</a> could prevent 1.4 million child deaths from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diarrhoea" title="Diarrhoea">diarrhoea</a> each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>In developing countries, 90% of all <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">municipal wastewater</a> still goes untreated into local rivers and streams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Some 50 countries, with roughly a third of the world's population, also suffer from medium or high <a href="/wiki/Water_scarcity" title="Water scarcity">water scarcity</a> and 17 of these extract more water annually than is recharged through their natural water cycles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The strain not only affects surface freshwater bodies like rivers and lakes, but it also degrades groundwater resources.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_uses">Human uses</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">Water supply</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Water_withdrawals_per_capita,_OWID.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Water_withdrawals_per_capita%2C_OWID.svg/350px-Water_withdrawals_per_capita%2C_OWID.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Water_withdrawals_per_capita%2C_OWID.svg/525px-Water_withdrawals_per_capita%2C_OWID.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Water_withdrawals_per_capita%2C_OWID.svg/700px-Water_withdrawals_per_capita%2C_OWID.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Water_withdrawals_per_capita,_OWID.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Total water withdrawals for agricultural, industrial and municipal purposes per capita, measured in cubic metres (m¬≥) per year in 2010<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span></h4>
<p>The most substantial human use of water is for agriculture, including irrigated agriculture, which accounts for as much as 80 to 90 percent of total human water consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> In the United States, 42% of freshwater withdrawn for use is for irrigation, but the vast majority of water "consumed" (used and not returned to the environment) goes to agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Estimated_use_of_water_in_the_United_States_in_2015_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Estimated_use_of_water_in_the_United_States_in_2015-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Access to fresh water is often taken for granted, especially in developed countries that have built sophisticated water systems for collecting, purifying, and delivering water, and removing wastewater. But growing economic, demographic, and climatic pressures are increasing concerns about water issues, leading to increasing competition for fixed water resources, giving rise to the concept of <a href="/wiki/Peak_water" title="Peak water">peak water</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> As populations and economies continue to grow, consumption of water-thirsty meat expands, and new demands rise for biofuels or new water-intensive industries, new water challenges are likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>An assessment of water management in agriculture was conducted in 2007 by the <a href="/wiki/International_Water_Management_Institute" title="International Water Management Institute">International Water Management Institute</a> in Sri Lanka to see if the world had sufficient water to provide food for its growing population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> It assessed the current availability of water for agriculture on a global scale and mapped out locations suffering from water scarcity. It found that a fifth of the world's people, more than 1.2 billion, live in areas of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_water_scarcity" title="Physical water scarcity">physical water scarcity</a>, where there is not enough water to meet all demands. A further 1.6 billion people live in areas experiencing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic_water_scarcity" title="Economic water scarcity">economic water scarcity</a>, where the lack of investment in water or insufficient human capacity make it impossible for authorities to satisfy the demand for water. The report found that it would be possible to produce the food required in the future, but that continuation of today's food production and environmental trends would lead to crises in many parts of the world. To avoid a global water crisis, farmers will have to strive to increase productivity to meet growing demands for food, while industries and cities find ways to use water more efficiently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Water scarcity is also caused by production of water intensive products. For example, <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>: 1¬†kg of cotton‚Äîequivalent of a pair of jeans‚Äîrequires 10.9 cubic meters (380¬†cu¬†ft) water to produce. While cotton accounts for 2.4% of world water use, the water is consumed in regions that are already at a risk of water shortage. Significant environmental damage has been caused: for example, the diversion of water by the former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Amu_Darya" title="Amu Darya">Amu Darya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syr_Darya" title="Syr Darya">Syr Darya</a> rivers to produce cotton was largely responsible for the disappearance of the <a href="/wiki/Aral_Sea" title="Aral Sea">Aral Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:32.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Water_requirement_per_tonne_of_food_product,_OWID.svg" title="Water requirement per tonne of food product"><img alt="Water requirement per tonne of food product" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="85" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Water_requirement_per_tonne_of_food_product%2C_OWID.svg/120px-Water_requirement_per_tonne_of_food_product%2C_OWID.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Water_requirement_per_tonne_of_food_product%2C_OWID.svg/180px-Water_requirement_per_tonne_of_food_product%2C_OWID.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Water_requirement_per_tonne_of_food_product%2C_OWID.svg/240px-Water_requirement_per_tonne_of_food_product%2C_OWID.svg.png 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Water requirement per tonne of food product
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_scientific_standard">As a scientific standard</span></h4>
<p>On 7 April 1795, the gram was defined in France to be equal to "the absolute weight of a volume of pure water equal to a cube of one-hundredth of a meter, and at the temperature of melting ice".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> For practical purposes though, a metallic reference standard was required, one thousand times more massive, the kilogram. Work was therefore commissioned to determine precisely the mass of one liter of water. In spite of the fact that the decreed definition of the gram specified water at 0¬†¬∞C (32¬†¬∞F)‚Äîa highly reproducible <i>temperature</i>‚Äîthe scientists chose to redefine the standard and to perform their measurements at the temperature of highest water <i>density</i>, which was measured at the time as 4¬†¬∞C (39¬†¬∞F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kelvin_temperature_scale" title="Kelvin temperature scale">Kelvin temperature scale</a> of the <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">SI</a> system was based on the <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> of water, defined as exactly 273.16¬†K (0.01¬†¬∞C; 32.02¬†¬∞F), but as of May 2019 is based on the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a> instead. The scale is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_temperature" title="Absolute temperature">absolute temperature</a> scale with the same increment as the Celsius temperature scale, which was originally defined according to the <a href="/wiki/Boiling_point" title="Boiling point">boiling point</a> (set to 100¬†¬∞C (212¬†¬∞F)) and <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">melting point</a> (set to 0¬†¬∞C (32¬†¬∞F)) of water.
</p><p>Natural water consists mainly of the isotopes hydrogen-1 and oxygen-16, but there is also a small quantity of heavier isotopes oxygen-18, oxygen-17, and hydrogen-2 (<a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">deuterium</a>). The percentage of the heavier isotopes is very small, but it still affects the properties of water. Water from rivers and lakes tends to contain less heavy isotopes than seawater. Therefore, standard water is defined in the <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Standard_Mean_Ocean_Water" title="Vienna Standard Mean Ocean Water">Vienna Standard Mean Ocean Water</a> specification.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="For_drinking">For drinking</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">Drinking water</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Humanitarian_aid_OCPA-2005-10-28-090517a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1701" data-file-width="2344" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Humanitarian_aid_OCPA-2005-10-28-090517a.jpg/220px-Humanitarian_aid_OCPA-2005-10-28-090517a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Humanitarian_aid_OCPA-2005-10-28-090517a.jpg/330px-Humanitarian_aid_OCPA-2005-10-28-090517a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Humanitarian_aid_OCPA-2005-10-28-090517a.jpg/440px-Humanitarian_aid_OCPA-2005-10-28-090517a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Humanitarian_aid_OCPA-2005-10-28-090517a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A young girl drinking <a href="/wiki/Bottled_water" title="Bottled water">bottled water</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2006_Global_Water_Availability.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="863" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/2006_Global_Water_Availability.svg/220px-2006_Global_Water_Availability.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/2006_Global_Water_Availability.svg/330px-2006_Global_Water_Availability.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/2006_Global_Water_Availability.svg/440px-2006_Global_Water_Availability.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2006_Global_Water_Availability.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Water availability: the fraction of the population using improved water sources by country</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roadside_fresh_water_outlet_from_glacier,_Nubra,_Ladakh.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="627" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Roadside_fresh_water_outlet_from_glacier%2C_Nubra%2C_Ladakh.jpg/220px-Roadside_fresh_water_outlet_from_glacier%2C_Nubra%2C_Ladakh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Roadside_fresh_water_outlet_from_glacier%2C_Nubra%2C_Ladakh.jpg/330px-Roadside_fresh_water_outlet_from_glacier%2C_Nubra%2C_Ladakh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Roadside_fresh_water_outlet_from_glacier%2C_Nubra%2C_Ladakh.jpg/440px-Roadside_fresh_water_outlet_from_glacier%2C_Nubra%2C_Ladakh.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roadside_fresh_water_outlet_from_glacier,_Nubra,_Ladakh.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roadside fresh water outlet from glacier, <a href="/wiki/Nubra" title="Nubra">Nubra</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Human_body" title="Human body">human body</a> contains from 55% to 78% water, depending on body size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> To function properly, the body requires between one and seven liters (0.22 and 1.54¬†imp¬†gal; 0.26 and 1.85¬†U.S.¬†gal)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> of water per day to avoid <a href="/wiki/Dehydration" title="Dehydration">dehydration</a>; the precise amount depends on the level of activity, temperature, humidity, and other factors. Most of this is ingested through foods or beverages other than drinking straight water. It is not clear how much water intake is needed by healthy people, though the British Dietetic Association advises that 2.5 liters of total water daily is the minimum to maintain proper hydration, including 1.8 liters (6 to 7 glasses) obtained directly from beverages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Medical literature favors a lower consumption, typically 1 liter of water for an average male, excluding extra requirements due to fluid loss from exercise or warm weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rhoades_2003_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rhoades_2003-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Healthy kidneys can excrete 0.8 to 1 liter of water per hour, but stress such as exercise can reduce this amount. People can drink far more water than necessary while exercising, putting them at risk of <a href="/wiki/Water_intoxication" title="Water intoxication">water intoxication</a> (hyperhydration), which can be fatal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> The popular claim that "a person should consume eight glasses of water per day" seems to have no real basis in science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Studies have shown that extra water intake, especially up to 500 milliliters (18¬†imp¬†fl¬†oz; 17¬†U.S.¬†fl¬†oz) at mealtime was associated with weight loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Adequate fluid intake is helpful in preventing constipation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DIN_4844-2_D-P005.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="574" data-file-width="574" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/DIN_4844-2_D-P005.svg/220px-DIN_4844-2_D-P005.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/DIN_4844-2_D-P005.svg/330px-DIN_4844-2_D-P005.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/DIN_4844-2_D-P005.svg/440px-DIN_4844-2_D-P005.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DIN_4844-2_D-P005.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hazard_symbol" title="Hazard symbol">Hazard symbol</a> for non-potable water</div></div></div>
<p>An original recommendation for water intake in 1945 by the Food and Nutrition Board of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._National_Research_Council" title="U.S. National Research Council">U.S. National Research Council</a> read: "An ordinary standard for diverse persons is 1 milliliter for each calorie of food. Most of this quantity is contained in prepared foods."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> The latest dietary reference intake report by the U.S. National Research Council in general recommended, based on the median total water intake from US survey data (including food sources): 3.7 liters (0.81¬†imp¬†gal; 0.98¬†U.S.¬†gal) for men and 2.7 liters (0.59¬†imp¬†gal; 0.71¬†U.S.¬†gal) of water total for women, noting that water contained in food provided approximately 19% of total water intake in the survey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Specifically, pregnant and <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding" title="Breastfeeding">breastfeeding</a> women need additional fluids to stay hydrated. The US <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Institute_of_Medicine" title="Institute of Medicine">Institute of Medicine</a> recommends that, on average, men consume 3 liters (0.66¬†imp¬†gal; 0.79¬†U.S.¬†gal) and women 2.2 liters (0.48¬†imp¬†gal; 0.58¬†U.S.¬†gal); pregnant women should increase intake to 2.4 liters (0.53¬†imp¬†gal; 0.63¬†U.S.¬†gal) and breastfeeding women should get 3 liters (12 cups), since an especially large amount of fluid is lost during nursing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> Also noted is that normally, about 20% of water intake comes from food, while the rest comes from drinking water and beverages (<a href="/wiki/Caffeine" title="Caffeine">caffeinated</a> included). Water is excreted from the body in multiple forms; through <a href="/wiki/Urine" title="Urine">urine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feces" title="Feces">feces</a>, through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sweat" title="Sweat">sweating</a>, and by exhalation of water vapor in the breath. With physical exertion and heat exposure, water loss will increase and daily fluid needs may increase as well.
</p><p>Humans require water with few impurities. Common impurities include metal salts and oxides, including copper, iron, calcium and lead,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> and/or harmful bacteria, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Vibrio" title="Vibrio">Vibrio</a></i>. Some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solutes" title="Solutes">solutes</a> are acceptable and even desirable for taste enhancement and to provide needed <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolytes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>The single largest (by volume) freshwater resource suitable for drinking is <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a> in Siberia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Washing">Washing</span></h4>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Washing" title="Washing">Washing</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Washing&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Woman_washing_hands.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="1993" decoding="async" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Woman_washing_hands.jpg/220px-Woman_washing_hands.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Woman_washing_hands.jpg/330px-Woman_washing_hands.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Woman_washing_hands.jpg/440px-Woman_washing_hands.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Woman_washing_hands.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A woman washes her hands with soap and water.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Washing" title="Washing">Washing</a> is a method of <a href="/wiki/Cleaning" title="Cleaning">cleaning</a>, usually with water and <a href="/wiki/Soap" title="Soap">soap</a> or <a href="/wiki/Detergent" title="Detergent">detergent</a>.  Washing and then rinsing both body and clothing is an essential part of good <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a> and health. <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Often people use soaps and detergents to assist in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emulsification" title="Emulsification">emulsification</a> of oils and dirt particles so they can be washed away. The soap can be applied directly, or with the aid of a <a href="/wiki/Towel" title="Towel">washcloth</a>.
</p><p>People wash themselves, or <a href="/wiki/Bathing" title="Bathing">bathe</a> periodically purification|religious ritual]] or therapeutic purposes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> or as a recreational activity.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, some people use a <a href="/wiki/Bidet" title="Bidet">bidet</a> to wash their external genitalia and the <a href="/wiki/Anal_hygiene" title="Anal hygiene">anal region</a> after using the <a href="/wiki/Toilet" title="Toilet">toilet</a>, instead of using <a href="/wiki/Toilet_paper" title="Toilet paper">toilet paper</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bidet" title="Bidet">bidet</a> is common in predominantly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic_countries" title="Catholic countries">Catholic countries</a> where water is considered essential for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anal_cleansing" title="Anal cleansing">anal cleansing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>More frequent is washing of just the <a href="/wiki/Hand" title="Hand">hands</a>, e.g. before and after preparing food and eating, after using the toilet, after handling something dirty, etc.  <a href="/wiki/Hand_washing" title="Hand washing">Hand washing</a> is important in reducing the spread of <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">germs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Also common is washing the face, which is done after waking up, or to keep oneself cool during the day. <a href="/wiki/Tooth_brushing" title="Tooth brushing">Brushing one's teeth</a> is also essential for hygiene and is a part of washing.
</p><p>'Washing' can also refer to the <a href="/wiki/Laundry" title="Laundry">washing of clothing</a> or other cloth items, like bedsheets, whether by hand or with a <a href="/wiki/Washing_machine" title="Washing machine">washing machine</a>. It can also refer to <a href="/wiki/Car_wash" title="Car wash">washing one's car</a>, by lathering the exterior with car soap, then rinsing it off with a hose, or <a href="/wiki/Dishwashing" title="Dishwashing">washing cookware</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Washer.600pix.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Washer.600pix.jpg/220px-Washer.600pix.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Washer.600pix.jpg/330px-Washer.600pix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Washer.600pix.jpg/440px-Washer.600pix.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Washer.600pix.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A private home <a href="/wiki/Washing_machine" title="Washing machine">washing machine</a></div></div></div>
Excessive washing may damage the hair, causing dandruff, or cause rough skin/skin lesions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span></h4>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">These paragraphs are an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Maritime_transport" title="Maritime transport">Maritime transport</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Maritime_transport&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<p><a href="/wiki/Maritime_transport" title="Maritime transport">Maritime transport</a> (or ocean transport) and hydraulic effluvial transport, or more generally waterborne transport, is the <a href="/wiki/Transport" title="Transport">transport</a> of people (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passengers" title="Passengers">passengers</a>) or goods (<a href="/wiki/Cargo" title="Cargo">cargo</a>) via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waterways" title="Waterways">waterways</a>. <a href="/wiki/Freight_transport" title="Freight transport">Freight transport</a> by sea has been widely used throughout <a href="/wiki/Recorded_history" title="Recorded history">recorded history</a>. The advent of aviation has diminished the importance of sea travel for passengers, though it is still popular for <a href="/wiki/Ferry" title="Ferry">short trips</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cruise_ship" title="Cruise ship">pleasure cruises</a>. Transport by water is cheaper than <a href="/wiki/Aviation" title="Aviation">transport by air</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> despite fluctuating <a href="/wiki/Exchange_rate" title="Exchange rate">exchange rates</a> and a fee placed on top of <a href="/wiki/Cargo" title="Cargo">freighting</a> charges for carrier companies known as the <a href="/wiki/Currency_adjustment_factor" title="Currency adjustment factor">currency adjustment factor</a>. Maritime transport accounts for roughly 80% of international trade, according to <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Conference_on_Trade_and_Development" title="United Nations Conference on Trade and Development">UNCTAD</a> in 2020.
</p><p>Maritime transport can be realized over any distance by boat, ship, sailboat or <a href="/wiki/Barge" title="Barge">barge</a>, over oceans and lakes, through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canal_age" title="Canal age">canals</a> or along rivers. Shipping may be for <a href="/wiki/Commerce" title="Commerce">commerce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Recreation" title="Recreation">recreation</a>, or for <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">military</a> purposes. While extensive inland shipping is less critical today, the major waterways of the world including many canals are still very important and are integral parts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Worldwide_economy" title="Worldwide economy">worldwide economies</a>. Particularly, especially any material can be moved by water; however, water transport becomes impractical when material delivery is  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time-critical" title="Time-critical">time-critical</a> such as various types of perishable <a href="/wiki/Produce" title="Produce">produce</a>. Still, water transport is highly cost effective with regular schedulable cargoes, such as trans-oceanic shipping of consumer products¬†‚Äì and especially for heavy loads or <a href="/wiki/Bulk_cargo" title="Bulk cargo">bulk cargos</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ores</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Grain" title="Grain">grains</a>. Arguably, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a> took place best where cheap water transport by canal, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Navigations" title="Navigations">navigations</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Freight_transport" title="Freight transport">shipping</a> by all types of watercraft on natural waterways supported cost-effective <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bulk_transport" title="Bulk transport">bulk transport</a>.
</p>
<a href="/wiki/Containerization" title="Containerization">Containerization</a> revolutionized maritime transport starting in the 1970s. "General cargo" includes goods packaged in boxes, cases, pallets, and barrels. When a cargo is carried in more than one mode, it is <a href="/wiki/Intermodal_freight_transport" title="Intermodal freight transport">intermodal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Co-modality" title="Co-modality">co-modal</a>.</div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_uses">Chemical uses</span></h4>
<p>Water is widely used in chemical reactions as a <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reactant" title="Reactant">reactant</a> and less commonly as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solute" title="Solute">solute</a> or catalyst. In inorganic reactions, water is a common solvent, dissolving many ionic compounds, as well as other polar compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_chalcogenide" title="Hydrogen chalcogenide">compounds closely related to water</a>. In organic reactions, it is not usually used as a reaction solvent, because it does not dissolve the reactants well and is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphoteric" title="Amphoteric">amphoteric</a> (acidic <i>and</i> basic) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleophilic" title="Nucleophilic">nucleophilic</a>. Nevertheless, these properties are sometimes desirable. Also, acceleration of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diels-Alder_reaction" title="Diels-Alder reaction">Diels-Alder reactions</a> by water has been observed. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supercritical_water" title="Supercritical water">Supercritical water</a> has recently been a topic of research. Oxygen-saturated supercritical water combusts organic pollutants efficiently. Water vapor is used for some processes in the chemical industry. An example is the production of acrylic acid from acrolein, propylene and propane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The possible effect of water in these reactions includes the physical-, chemical interaction of water with the catalyst and the chemical reaction of water with the reaction intermediates.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Heat_exchange">Heat exchange</span></h4>
<p>Water and steam are a common fluid used for <a href="/wiki/Heat_exchanger" title="Heat exchanger">heat exchange</a>, due to its availability and high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heat_capacity_of_water" title="Heat capacity of water">heat capacity</a>, both for cooling and heating. Cool water may even be naturally available from a lake or the sea. It's especially effective to transport heat through <a href="/wiki/Vaporization" title="Vaporization">vaporization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condensation</a> of water because of its large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latent_heat_of_vaporization" title="Latent heat of vaporization">latent heat of vaporization</a>. A disadvantage is that metals commonly found in industries such as steel and copper are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidized</a> faster by untreated water and steam. In almost all <a href="/wiki/Thermal_power_station" title="Thermal power station">thermal power stations</a>, water is used as the working fluid (used in a closed-loop between boiler, steam turbine, and condenser), and the coolant (used to exchange the waste heat to a water body or carry it away by <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Cooling_tower" title="Cooling tower">cooling tower</a>). In the United States, cooling power plants is the largest use of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Water_Use_in_the_United_States_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Water_Use_in_the_United_States-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> industry, water can also be used as a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">neutron moderator</a>. In most <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactors</a>, water is both a coolant and a moderator. This provides something of a passive safety measure, as removing the water from the reactor also <a href="/wiki/Void_coefficient" title="Void coefficient">slows the nuclear reaction down</a>. However other methods are favored for stopping a reaction and it is preferred to keep the nuclear core covered with water so as to ensure adequate cooling.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fire_considerations">Fire considerations</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MH-60S_Helicopter_dumps_water_onto_Fire.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/MH-60S_Helicopter_dumps_water_onto_Fire.jpg/220px-MH-60S_Helicopter_dumps_water_onto_Fire.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/MH-60S_Helicopter_dumps_water_onto_Fire.jpg/330px-MH-60S_Helicopter_dumps_water_onto_Fire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/MH-60S_Helicopter_dumps_water_onto_Fire.jpg/440px-MH-60S_Helicopter_dumps_water_onto_Fire.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MH-60S_Helicopter_dumps_water_onto_Fire.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Water is used for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fire_fighting" title="Fire fighting">fighting</a> <a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">wildfires</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Water has a high heat of vaporization and is relatively inert, which makes it a good <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fire_fighting#Use_of_water" title="Fire fighting">fire extinguishing</a> fluid. The evaporation of water carries heat away from the fire. It is dangerous to use water on fires involving oils and organic solvents because many organic materials float on water and the water tends to spread the burning liquid.
</p><p>Use of water in fire fighting should also take into account the hazards of a <a href="/wiki/Steam_explosion" title="Steam explosion">steam explosion</a>, which may occur when water is used on very hot fires in confined spaces, and of a hydrogen explosion, when substances which react with water, such as certain metals or hot carbon such as coal, <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a> graphite, decompose the water, producing <a href="/wiki/Water_gas" title="Water gas">water gas</a>.
</p><p>The power of such explosions was seen in the <a href="/wiki/Chernobyl_disaster" title="Chernobyl disaster">Chernobyl disaster</a>, although the water involved in this case did not come from fire-fighting but from the reactor's own water cooling system. A steam explosion occurred when the extreme overheating of the core caused water to flash into steam. A hydrogen explosion may have occurred as a result of a reaction between steam and hot <a href="/wiki/Zirconium" title="Zirconium">zirconium</a>.
</p><p>Some metallic oxides, most notably those of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkali_metals" title="Alkali metals">alkali metals</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkaline_earth_metals" title="Alkaline earth metals">alkaline earth metals</a>, produce so much heat on reaction with water that a fire hazard can develop. The alkaline earth oxide <a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxide" title="Calcium oxide">quicklime</a> is a mass-produced substance that is often transported in paper bags. If these are soaked through, they may ignite as their contents react with water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Recreation">Recreation</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_sport_(recreation)" title="Water sport (recreation)">Water sport (recreation)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Johny_Cay.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2674" data-file-width="4019" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Johny_Cay.jpg/220px-Johny_Cay.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Johny_Cay.jpg/330px-Johny_Cay.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Johny_Cay.jpg/440px-Johny_Cay.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Johny_Cay.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/San_Andr%C3%A9s_(island)" title="San Andr√©s (island)">San Andr√©s island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a></div></div></div>
<p>Humans use water for many recreational purposes, as well as for exercising and for sports. Some of these include swimming, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waterskiing" title="Waterskiing">waterskiing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boating" title="Boating">boating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surfing" title="Surfing">surfing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Underwater_diving" title="Underwater diving">diving</a>. In addition, some sports, like <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">ice hockey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ice_skating" title="Ice skating">ice skating</a>, are played on ice. Lakesides, beaches and <a href="/wiki/Water_park" title="Water park">water parks</a> are popular places for people to go to relax and enjoy recreation. Many find the sound and appearance of flowing water to be calming, and fountains and other flowing water structures are popular decorations. Some keep fish and other flora and fauna inside <a href="/wiki/Aquarium" title="Aquarium">aquariums</a> or ponds for show, fun, and companionship. Humans also use water for snow sports such as <a href="/wiki/Skiing" title="Skiing">skiing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sledding" title="Sledding">sledding</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snowmobiling" title="Snowmobiling">snowmobiling</a> or <a href="/wiki/Snowboarding" title="Snowboarding">snowboarding</a>, which require the water to be frozen.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_industry">Water industry</span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Water_industry" title="Water industry">water industry</a> provides drinking water and <a href="/wiki/Wastewater" title="Wastewater">wastewater</a> services (including <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">sewage treatment</a>) to households and industry. <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">Water supply</a> facilities include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_well" title="Water well">water wells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cistern" title="Cistern">cisterns</a> for <a href="/wiki/Rainwater_harvesting" title="Rainwater harvesting">rainwater harvesting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_network" title="Water supply network">water supply networks</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Water_purification" title="Water purification">water purification</a> facilities, <a href="/wiki/Water_tank" title="Water tank">water tanks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_tower" title="Water tower">water towers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_pipe" title="Water pipe">water pipes</a> including old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aqueduct_(watercourse)" title="Aqueduct (watercourse)">aqueducts</a>. <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_water_generator" title="Atmospheric water generator">Atmospheric water generators</a> are in development.
</p><p>Drinking water is often collected at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spring_(hydrosphere)" title="Spring (hydrosphere)">springs</a>, extracted from artificial <a href="/wiki/Boring_(earth)" title="Boring (earth)">borings</a> (wells) in the ground, or pumped from lakes and rivers. Building more wells in adequate places is thus a possible way to produce more water, assuming the aquifers can supply an adequate flow. Other water sources include rainwater collection. Water may require purification for human consumption. This may involve the removal of undissolved substances, dissolved substances and harmful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbe" title="Microbe">microbes</a>. Popular methods are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filter_(water)" title="Filter (water)">filtering</a> with sand which only removes undissolved material, while <a href="/wiki/Water_chlorination" title="Water chlorination">chlorination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boiling" title="Boiling">boiling</a> kill harmful microbes. <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">Distillation</a> does all three functions. More advanced techniques exist, such as <a href="/wiki/Reverse_osmosis" title="Reverse osmosis">reverse osmosis</a>. <a href="/wiki/Desalination" title="Desalination">Desalination</a> of abundant <a href="/wiki/Seawater" title="Seawater">seawater</a> is a more expensive solution used in coastal <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a> <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climates</a>.
</p><p>The distribution of drinking water is done through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Municipal_water_system" title="Municipal water system">municipal water systems</a>, tanker delivery or as <a href="/wiki/Bottled_water" title="Bottled water">bottled water</a>. Governments in many countries have programs to distribute water to the needy at no charge.
</p><p>Reducing usage by using drinking (potable) water only for human consumption is another option. In some cities such as Hong Kong, seawater is extensively used for flushing toilets citywide in order to <a href="/wiki/Water_conservation" title="Water conservation">conserve freshwater resources</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">Polluting water</a> may be the biggest single misuse of water; to the extent that a pollutant limits other uses of the water, it becomes a waste of the resource, regardless of benefits to the polluter. Like other types of pollution, this does not enter standard accounting of market costs, being conceived as <a href="/wiki/Externality" title="Externality">externalities</a> for which the market cannot account. Thus other people pay the price of water pollution, while the private firms' profits are not redistributed to the local population, victims of this pollution. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pharmaceuticals" title="Pharmaceuticals">Pharmaceuticals</a> consumed by humans often end up in the waterways and can have detrimental effects on <a href="/wiki/Marine_biology" title="Marine biology">aquatic</a> life if they <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">bioaccumulate</a> and if they are not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biodegradable" title="Biodegradable">biodegradable</a>.
</p><p>Municipal and <a href="/wiki/Industrial_wastewater_treatment" title="Industrial wastewater treatment">industrial wastewater</a> are typically treated at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wastewater_treatment_plant" title="Wastewater treatment plant">wastewater treatment plants</a>. Mitigation of polluted <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">surface runoff</a> is addressed through a variety of prevention and treatment techniques. (<i>See</i> <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff#Mitigation_and_treatment" title="Surface runoff">Surface runoff#Mitigation and treatment</a>.)
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1021810730" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="mod-gallery mod-gallery-default mod-gallery-center"><div class="main"><div><ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional nochecker bordered-images whitebg">

</p>
