<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_applications">Industrial applications</span></h4>
<p>Many industrial processes rely on reactions using chemicals dissolved in water, suspension of solids in water <a href="/wiki/Slurry" title="Slurry">slurries</a> or using water to dissolve and extract substances, or to wash products or process equipment. Processes such as <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mining</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_pulping" title="Chemical pulping">chemical pulping</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pulp_bleaching&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pulp bleaching (page does not exist)">pulp bleaching</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paper_manufacturing" title="Paper manufacturing">paper manufacturing</a>, textile production, dyeing, printing, and cooling of power plants use large amounts of water, requiring a dedicated water source, and often cause significant water pollution.
</p><p>Water is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_generation" title="Power generation">power generation</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">Hydroelectricity</a> is electricity obtained from <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a>. Hydroelectric power comes from water driving a water turbine connected to a generator. Hydroelectricity is a low-cost, non-polluting, renewable energy source. The energy is supplied by the motion of water. Typically a dam is constructed on a river, creating an artificial lake behind it. Water flowing out of the lake is forced through turbines that turn generators.
</p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left:auto;margin-right:auto;overflow:hidden;width:auto;max-width:808px"><div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize" style="overflow:auto"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:200407-sandouping-sanxiadaba-4.med.jpg" title="Three Gorges Dam is the largest hydro-electric power station."><img alt="" data-file-height="1228" data-file-width="4096" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/200407-sandouping-sanxiadaba-4.med.jpg/800px-200407-sandouping-sanxiadaba-4.med.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/200407-sandouping-sanxiadaba-4.med.jpg/1200px-200407-sandouping-sanxiadaba-4.med.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/200407-sandouping-sanxiadaba-4.med.jpg/1600px-200407-sandouping-sanxiadaba-4.med.jpg 2x" width="800"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:200407-sandouping-sanxiadaba-4.med.jpg" title="File:200407-sandouping-sanxiadaba-4.med.jpg"> </a></div><a href="/wiki/Three_Gorges_Dam" title="Three Gorges Dam">Three Gorges Dam</a> is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_the_largest_hydroelectric_power_stations" title="List of the largest hydroelectric power stations">largest hydro-electric power station</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Pressurized water is used in <a href="/wiki/Hydrodemolition" title="Hydrodemolition">water blasting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_jet_cutter" title="Water jet cutter">water jet cutters</a>. Also, high pressure water guns are used for precise cutting. It works very well, is relatively safe, and is not harmful to the environment. It is also used in the cooling of machinery to prevent overheating, or prevent saw blades from overheating.
</p><p>Water is also used in many industrial processes and machines, such as the <a href="/wiki/Steam_turbine" title="Steam turbine">steam turbine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heat_exchanger" title="Heat exchanger">heat exchanger</a>, in addition to its use as a chemical <a href="/wiki/Solvent" title="Solvent">solvent</a>. Discharge of untreated water from industrial uses is <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">pollution</a>. Pollution includes discharged solutes (chemical pollution) and discharged coolant water (<a href="/wiki/Thermal_pollution" title="Thermal pollution">thermal pollution</a>). Industry requires pure water for many applications and utilizes a variety of purification techniques both in water supply and discharge.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_processing">Food processing</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cuisson_des_pates.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cuisson_des_pates.jpg/220px-Cuisson_des_pates.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cuisson_des_pates.jpg/330px-Cuisson_des_pates.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cuisson_des_pates.jpg/440px-Cuisson_des_pates.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cuisson_des_pates.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Water can be used to cook foods such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noodles" title="Noodles">noodles</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sterilewater.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2213" data-file-width="1542" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Sterilewater.jpg/170px-Sterilewater.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Sterilewater.jpg/255px-Sterilewater.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Sterilewater.jpg/340px-Sterilewater.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sterilewater.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sterile water for injection</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Boiling" title="Boiling">Boiling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steaming" title="Steaming">steaming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Simmering" title="Simmering">simmering</a> are popular cooking methods that often require immersing food in water or its gaseous state, steam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Water is also used for <a href="/wiki/Dishwashing" title="Dishwashing">dishwashing</a>. Water also plays many critical roles within the field of <a href="/wiki/Food_science" title="Food science">food science</a>.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solutes" title="Solutes">Solutes</a> such as salts and sugars found in water affect the physical properties of water. The boiling and freezing points of water are affected by solutes, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_pressure" title="Air pressure">air pressure</a>, which is in turn affected by altitude. Water boils at lower temperatures with the lower air pressure that occurs at higher elevations. One <a href="/wiki/Mole_(unit)" title="Mole (unit)">mole</a> of sucrose (sugar) per kilogram of water raises the boiling point of water by 0.51¬†¬∞C (0.918¬†¬∞F), and one mole of salt per kg raises the boiling point by 1.02¬†¬∞C (1.836¬†¬∞F); similarly, increasing the number of dissolved particles lowers water's freezing point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vaclacik_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-vaclacik-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p><p>Solutes in water also affect water activity that affects many chemical reactions and the growth of microbes in food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deman_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-deman-125">[125]</a></sup> Water activity can be described as a ratio of the vapor pressure of water in a solution to the vapor pressure of pure water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vaclacik_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-vaclacik-124">[124]</a></sup> Solutes in water lower water activity‚Äîthis is important to know because most bacterial growth ceases at low levels of water activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deman_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-deman-125">[125]</a></sup> Not only does microbial growth affect the safety of food, but also the preservation and shelf life of food.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_hardness" title="Water hardness">Water hardness</a> is also a critical factor in food processing and may be altered or treated by using a chemical ion exchange system. It can dramatically affect the quality of a product, as well as playing a role in sanitation. Water hardness is classified based on concentration of calcium carbonate the water contains. Water is classified as soft if it contains less than 100¬†mg/L (UK)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DEFRA_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-DEFRA-126">[126]</a></sup> or less than 60¬†mg/L (US).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a report published by the Water Footprint organization in 2010, a single kilogram of beef requires 15¬†thousand liters (3.3<span style="margin-left:0.2em">√ó<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>3</sup>¬†imp¬†gal; 4.0<span style="margin-left:0.2em">√ó<span style="margin-left:0.1em">10</span></span><s style="display:none">^</s><sup>3</sup>¬†U.S.¬†gal) of water; however, the authors also make clear that this is a global average and circumstantial factors determine the amount of water used in beef production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_use">Medical use</span></h4>
<p>Water for injection is on the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization%27s_list_of_essential_medicines" title="World Health Organization's list of essential medicines">list of essential medicines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_in_nature">Distribution in nature</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_universe">In the universe</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Band_5_ALMA_receiver.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="886" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Band_5_ALMA_receiver.jpg/220px-Band_5_ALMA_receiver.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Band_5_ALMA_receiver.jpg/330px-Band_5_ALMA_receiver.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Band_5_ALMA_receiver.jpg/440px-Band_5_ALMA_receiver.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Band_5_ALMA_receiver.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Band 5 <a href="/wiki/Atacama_Large_Millimeter_Array" title="Atacama Large Millimeter Array">ALMA</a> receiver is an instrument specifically designed to detect water in the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Much of the universe's water is produced as a byproduct of <a href="/wiki/Star_formation" title="Star formation">star formation</a>. The formation of stars is accompanied by a strong outward wind of gas and dust. When this outflow of material eventually impacts the surrounding gas, the shock waves that are created compress and heat the gas. The water observed is quickly produced in this warm dense gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 22 July 2011, a report described the discovery of a gigantic cloud of water vapor containing "140 trillion times more water than all of Earth's oceans combined" around a <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">quasar</a> located 12 billion light years from Earth. According to the researchers, the "discovery shows that water has been prevalent in the universe for nearly its entire existence".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clavin_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clavin-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-water_vapor_cloud_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-water_vapor_cloud-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>Water has been detected in <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_cloud" title="Interstellar cloud">interstellar clouds</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> Water probably exists in abundance in other galaxies, too, because its components, hydrogen, and oxygen, are among the most abundant elements in the universe. Based on models of the <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">formation and evolution of the Solar System</a> and that of other star systems, most other <a href="/wiki/Planetary_system" title="Planetary system">planetary systems</a> are likely to have similar ingredients.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_vapor">Water vapor</span></h4>
<p>Water is present as vapor in:
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_atmosphere" title="Solar atmosphere">Atmosphere of the Sun</a>: in detectable trace amounts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solanki1994_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Solanki1994-135">[135]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mercury" title="Atmosphere of Mercury">Atmosphere of Mercury</a>: 3.4%, and large amounts of water in <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury's</a> <a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">exosphere</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planetary_society_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-planetary_society-136">[136]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Venus" title="Atmosphere of Venus">Atmosphere of Venus</a>: 0.002%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bertaux2007_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bertaux2007-137">[137]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a>: ‚âà0.40% over full atmosphere, typically 1‚Äì4% at surface; as well as <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_the_Moon" title="Atmosphere of the Moon">that of the Moon</a> in trace amounts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sridharan2010_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sridharan2010-138">[138]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Mars" title="Atmosphere of Mars">Atmosphere of Mars</a>: 0.03%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rapp2012_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rapp2012-139">[139]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Ceres" title="Atmosphere of Ceres">Atmosphere of Ceres</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuppers2014_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuppers2014-140">[140]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Jupiter" title="Atmosphere of Jupiter">Atmosphere of Jupiter</a>: 0.0004%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> ‚Äì in <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">ices</a> only; and that of its moon <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20131212-EU_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20131212-EU-142">[142]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Saturn" title="Atmosphere of Saturn">Atmosphere of Saturn</a> ‚Äì in <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">ices</a> only; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enceladus_(moon)" title="Enceladus (moon)">Enceladus</a>: 91%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-143">[143]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Dione_(moon)" title="Dione (moon)">Dione</a> (exosphere)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Uranus" title="Atmosphere of Uranus">Atmosphere of Uranus</a> ‚Äì in trace amounts below 50 bar</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Neptune" title="Atmosphere of Neptune">Atmosphere of Neptune</a> ‚Äì found in the deeper layers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubbard_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-hubbard-144">[144]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planet" title="Extrasolar planet">Extrasolar planet</a> atmospheres: including those of <a href="/wiki/HD_189733_b" title="HD 189733 b">HD 189733 b</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/HD_209458_b" title="HD 209458 b">HD 209458 b</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Space.com_water_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Space.com_water-146">[146]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tau_Bo%C3%B6tis_b" title="Tau Bo√∂tis b">Tau Bo√∂tis b</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/HAT-P-11b" title="HAT-P-11b">HAT-P-11b</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140924_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140924-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanslmeier2010_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hanslmeier2010-149">[149]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/XO-1b" title="XO-1b">XO-1b</a>, <a href="/wiki/WASP-12b" title="WASP-12b">WASP-12b</a>, <a href="/wiki/WASP-17b" title="WASP-17b">WASP-17b</a>, and <a href="/wiki/WASP-19b" title="WASP-19b">WASP-19b</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20131203_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20131203-150">[150]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Stellar_atmosphere" title="Stellar atmosphere">Stellar atmospheres</a>: not limited to cooler stars and even detected in giant hot stars such as <a href="/wiki/Betelgeuse" title="Betelgeuse">Betelgeuse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mu_Cephei" title="Mu Cephei">Mu Cephei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antares" title="Antares">Antares</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arcturus" title="Arcturus">Arcturus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanslmeier2010_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hanslmeier2010-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lund_Observatory_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lund_Observatory-151">[151]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circumstellar_disk" title="Circumstellar disk">Circumstellar disks</a>: including those of more than half of <a href="/wiki/T_Tauri_star" title="T Tauri star">T Tauri stars</a> such as <a href="/wiki/AA_Tauri" title="AA Tauri">AA Tauri</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanslmeier2010_149-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hanslmeier2010-149">[149]</a></sup> as well as <a href="/wiki/TW_Hydrae" title="TW Hydrae">TW Hydrae</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IRC_%2B10216" title="IRC +10216">IRC +10216</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/APM_08279%2B5255" title="APM 08279+5255">APM 08279+5255</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clavin_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clavin-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-water_vapor_cloud_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-water_vapor_cloud-133">[133]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/VY_Canis_Majoris" title="VY Canis Majoris">VY Canis Majoris</a> and <a href="/wiki/S_Persei" title="S Persei">S Persei</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lund_Observatory_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lund_Observatory-151">[151]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Liquid_water">Liquid water</span></h4>
<p>Liquid water is present on Earth, covering 71% of its surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSS_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-WSS-3">[3]</a></sup> Liquid water is also occasionally present in small amounts <a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">on Mars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Scientists believe liquid water is present in the Saturnian moons of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enceladus_(moon)" title="Enceladus (moon)">Enceladus</a>, as a 10-kilometre thick ocean approximately 30‚Äì40 kilometres below Enceladus' south polar surface,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140403_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140403-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20140404_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20140404-157">[157]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, as a subsurface layer, possibly mixed with <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> Jupiter's moon <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a> has surface characteristics which suggest a subsurface liquid water ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Liquid water may also exist on Jupiter's moon <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a> as a layer sandwiched between high pressure ice and rock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_ice">Water ice</span></h4>
<p>Water is present as ice on:
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1234" data-file-width="1690" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg/220px-South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg/330px-South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg/440px-South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:South_Polar_Cap_of_Mars_during_Martian_South_summer_2000.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>South polar ice cap of Mars during Martian south summer 2000</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Water_on_Mars" title="Water on Mars">Mars</a>: under the regolith and at the poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></li>
<li>Earth‚ÄìMoon system: mainly as <a href="/wiki/Ice_sheet" title="Ice sheet">ice sheets</a> on Earth and in Lunar craters and volcanic rocks<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> NASA reported the detection of water molecules by NASA's Moon Mineralogy Mapper aboard the Indian Space Research Organization's Chandrayaan-1 spacecraft in September 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCord2005-jgrp_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCord2005-jgrp-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2005_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas2005-166">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carey2006_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carey2006-167">[167]</a></sup></li>
<li>Jupiter's moons: <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>'s surface and also that of <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150315_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150315-168">[168]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuskov2005_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuskov2005-169">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-170">[170]</a></sup></li>
<li>Saturn: in the <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">planet's ring system</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sparrow_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sparrow-171">[171]</a></sup> and on the surface and mantle of <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobie_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tobie-172">[172]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enceladus_(moon)" title="Enceladus (moon)">Enceladus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verbiscer_et_al._2007_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Verbiscer_et_al._2007-173">[173]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>‚Äì<a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a> system<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sparrow_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sparrow-171">[171]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comets" title="Comets">Comets</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> and other related <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a> objects<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>And is also likely present on:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>'s poles<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tethys_(moon)" title="Tethys (moon)">Tethys</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Exotic_forms">Exotic forms</span></h4>
<p>Water and other <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">volatiles</a> probably comprise much of the internal structures of <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> and the water in the deeper layers may be in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionic_water" title="Ionic water">ionic water</a> in which the molecules break down into a soup of hydrogen and oxygen ions, and deeper still as <a href="/wiki/Superionic_water" title="Superionic water">superionic water</a> in which the oxygen crystallizes, but the hydrogen ions float about freely within the oxygen lattice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newscientist.com_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-newscientist.com-179">[179]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_and_planetary_habitability">Water and planetary habitability</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Water_distribution_on_Earth" title="Water distribution on Earth">Water distribution on Earth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">Planetary habitability</a></div>
<p>The existence of liquid water, and to a lesser extent its gaseous and solid forms, on Earth are vital to the existence of <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">life on Earth</a> as we know it. The Earth is located in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitable_zone" title="Habitable zone">habitable zone</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>; if it were slightly closer to or farther from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> (about 5%, or about 8 million kilometers), the conditions which allow the three forms to be present simultaneously would be far less likely to exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>Earth's <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> allows it to hold an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_body_atmosphere" title="Celestial body atmosphere">atmosphere</a>. Water vapor and carbon dioxide in the atmosphere provide a temperature buffer (<a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a>) which helps maintain a relatively steady surface temperature. If Earth were smaller, a thinner atmosphere would allow temperature extremes, thus preventing the accumulation of water except in <a href="/wiki/Polar_ice_cap" title="Polar ice cap">polar ice caps</a> (as on <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The surface temperature of Earth has been relatively constant through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geologic_time" title="Geologic time">geologic time</a> despite varying levels of incoming solar radiation (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insolation" title="Insolation">insolation</a>), indicating that a dynamic process governs Earth's temperature via a combination of greenhouse gases and surface or atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>. This proposal is known as the <a href="/wiki/Gaia_hypothesis" title="Gaia hypothesis">Gaia hypothesis</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The state of water on a planet depends on ambient pressure, which is determined by the planet's gravity. If a planet is sufficiently massive, the water on it may be solid even at high temperatures, because of the high pressure caused by gravity, as it was observed on exoplanets <a href="/wiki/Gliese_436_b" title="Gliese 436 b">Gliese 436 b</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GJ_1214_b" title="GJ 1214 b">GJ 1214 b</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Law.2C_politics.2C_and_crisis"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Law,_politics,_and_crisis">Law, politics, and crisis</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_law" title="Water law">Water law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_right" title="Water right">Water right</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_crisis" title="Water crisis">Water crisis</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Update plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="Ambox current red Americas.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red_Americas.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2020</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Access_to_drinking_water_in_third_world.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="320" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Access_to_drinking_water_in_third_world.svg/300px-Access_to_drinking_water_in_third_world.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Access_to_drinking_water_in_third_world.svg/450px-Access_to_drinking_water_in_third_world.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Access_to_drinking_water_in_third_world.svg/600px-Access_to_drinking_water_in_third_world.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Access_to_drinking_water_in_third_world.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An estimate of the proportion of people in developing countries with access to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potable_water" title="Potable water">potable water</a> 1970‚Äì2000</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Water_politics" title="Water politics">Water politics</a> is politics affected by water and <a href="/wiki/Water_resources" title="Water resources">water resources</a>. For this reason, water is a strategic resource in the globe and an important element in many political conflicts. It causes health impacts and damage to biodiversity.
</p><p>Access to safe drinking water has improved over the last decades in almost every part of the world, but approximately one billion people still lack access to safe water and over 2.5 billion lack access to adequate <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN-184">[184]</a></sup> However, some observers have estimated that by 2025 more than half of the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> will be facing water-based vulnerability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> A report, issued in November 2009, suggests that by 2030, in some developing regions of the world, water demand will exceed supply by 50%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup>
</p><p>1.6 billion people have gained access to a safe water source since 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> The proportion of people in <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a> with <a href="/wiki/WASH" title="WASH">access to safe water</a> is calculated to have improved from 30% in 1970<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lomborg_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-lomborg-188">[188]</a></sup> to 71% in 1990, 79% in 2000 and 84% in 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-UN-184">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2006 United Nations report stated that "there is enough water for everyone", but that access to it is hampered by mismanagement and corruption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> In addition, global initiatives to improve the efficiency of aid delivery, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paris_Declaration_on_Aid_Effectiveness" title="Paris Declaration on Aid Effectiveness">Paris Declaration on Aid Effectiveness</a>, have not been taken up by water sector donors as effectively as they have in education and health, potentially leaving multiple donors working on overlapping projects and recipient governments without empowerment to act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>The authors of the 2007 <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_Assessment_of_Water_Management_in_Agriculture" title="Comprehensive Assessment of Water Management in Agriculture">Comprehensive Assessment of Water Management in Agriculture</a> cited poor governance as one reason for some forms of water scarcity. Water governance is the set of formal and informal processes through which decisions related to water management are made. Good water governance is primarily about knowing what processes work best in a particular physical and socioeconomic context. Mistakes have sometimes been made by trying to apply 'blueprints' that work in the developed world to developing world locations and contexts. The Mekong river is one example; a review by the <a href="/wiki/International_Water_Management_Institute" title="International Water Management Institute">International Water Management Institute</a> of policies in six countries that rely on the Mekong river for water found that thorough and transparent cost-benefit analyses and environmental impact assessments were rarely undertaken. They also discovered that Cambodia's draft water law was much more complex than it needed to be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/UN_World_Water_Development_Report" title="UN World Water Development Report">UN World Water Development Report</a> (WWDR, 2003) from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Water_Assessment_Program" title="World Water Assessment Program">World Water Assessment Program</a> indicates that, in the next 20 years, the quantity of water available to everyone is predicted to decrease by 30%. 40% of the world's inhabitants currently have insufficient fresh water for minimal <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a>. More than 2.2 million people died in 2000 from <a href="/wiki/Waterborne_diseases" title="Waterborne diseases">waterborne diseases</a> (related to the consumption of contaminated water) or drought. In 2004, the UK charity <a href="/wiki/WaterAid" title="WaterAid">WaterAid</a> reported that a child dies every 15 seconds from easily preventable water-related diseases; often this means lack of <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage</a> disposal.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Organizations concerned with water protection include the <a href="/wiki/International_Water_Association" title="International Water Association">International Water Association</a> (IWA), WaterAid, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_1st" title="Water 1st">Water 1st</a>, and the American Water Resources Association. The <a href="/wiki/International_Water_Management_Institute" title="International Water Management Institute">International Water Management Institute</a> undertakes projects with the aim of using effective water management to reduce poverty. Water related conventions are <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Convention_to_Combat_Desertification" title="United Nations Convention to Combat Desertification">United Nations Convention to Combat Desertification</a> (UNCCD), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Convention_for_the_Prevention_of_Pollution_from_Ships" title="International Convention for the Prevention of Pollution from Ships">International Convention for the Prevention of Pollution from Ships</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Convention_on_the_Law_of_the_Sea" title="United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea">United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ramsar_Convention" title="Ramsar Convention">Ramsar Convention</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Day_for_Water" title="World Day for Water">World Day for Water</a> takes place on 22 March<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/World_Oceans_Day" title="World Oceans Day">World Oceans Day</a> on 8 June.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inda_Abba_Hadera_holy_water.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3096" data-file-width="4128" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Inda_Abba_Hadera_holy_water.jpg/220px-Inda_Abba_Hadera_holy_water.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Inda_Abba_Hadera_holy_water.jpg/330px-Inda_Abba_Hadera_holy_water.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Inda_Abba_Hadera_holy_water.jpg/440px-Inda_Abba_Hadera_holy_water.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inda_Abba_Hadera_holy_water.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>People come to Inda Abba Hadera spring (<a href="/wiki/Inda_Sillasie" title="Inda Sillasie">Inda Sillasie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>) to wash in holy water</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_and_religion" title="Water and religion">Water and religion</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sacred_waters" title="Sacred waters">Sacred waters</a></div>
<p>Water is considered a purifier in most religions. Faiths that incorporate ritual washing (<a href="/wiki/Ritual_purification" title="Ritual purification">ablution</a>) include <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rastafari_movement" title="Rastafari movement">Rastafari movement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wicca" title="Wicca">Wicca</a>. Immersion (or <a href="/wiki/Aspersion" title="Aspersion">aspersion</a> or <a href="/wiki/Affusion" title="Affusion">affusion</a>) of a person in water is a central <a href="/wiki/Sacrament" title="Sacrament">sacrament</a> of Christianity (where it is called <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a>); it is also a part of the practice of other religions, including Islam (<i><a href="/wiki/Ghusl" title="Ghusl">Ghusl</a></i>), Judaism (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mikvah" title="Mikvah">mikvah</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Amrit_Sanskar" title="Amrit Sanskar">Amrit Sanskar</a></i>). In addition, a ritual bath in pure water is performed for the dead in many religions including Islam and Judaism. In Islam, the five daily prayers can be done in most cases after washing certain parts of the body using clean water (<i><a href="/wiki/Wudu" title="Wudu">wudu</a></i>), unless water is unavailable (see <i><a href="/wiki/Tayammum" title="Tayammum">Tayammum</a></i>). In Shinto, water is used in almost all rituals to cleanse a person or an area (e.g., in the ritual of <i><a href="/wiki/Misogi" title="Misogi">misogi</a></i>).
</p><p>In Christianity, <a href="/wiki/Holy_water" title="Holy water">holy water</a> is water that has been sanctified by a priest for the purpose of <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blessing_(Roman_Catholic_Church)" title="Blessing (Roman Catholic Church)">blessing</a> of persons, places, and objects, or as a means of repelling evil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>, water (<i><a href="/wiki/Aban" title="Aban">ƒÅb</a></i>) is respected as the source of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Icosahedron-spinoza.jpg"><img alt="Icosahedron as a part of Spinoza monument in Amsterdam." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3861" data-file-width="3861" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Icosahedron-spinoza.jpg/220px-Icosahedron-spinoza.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Icosahedron-spinoza.jpg/330px-Icosahedron-spinoza.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Icosahedron-spinoza.jpg/440px-Icosahedron-spinoza.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Icosahedron-spinoza.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Icosahedron" title="Icosahedron">Icosahedron</a> as a part of <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Spinoza</a> monument in <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Ancient Greek philosopher <a href="/wiki/Empedocles" title="Empedocles">Empedocles</a> saw <a href="/wiki/Water_(classical_element)" title="Water (classical element)">water</a> as one of the four <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_elements" title="Classical elements">classical elements</a> (along with fire, earth, and <a href="/wiki/Air_(classical_element)" title="Air (classical element)">air</a>), and regarded it as an <a href="/wiki/Ylem" title="Ylem">ylem</a>, or basic substance of the universe. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thales" title="Thales">Thales</a>, whom Aristotle portrayed as an astronomer and an engineer, theorized that the earth, which is denser than water, emerged from the water. Thales, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monist" title="Monist">monist</a>, believed further that all things are made from water. <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> believed that the shape of water is an <a href="/wiki/Icosahedron" title="Icosahedron">icosahedron</a> ‚Äì flowing easily compared to the cube-shaped earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup>
</p><p>The theory of the <a href="/wiki/Humorism" title="Humorism">four bodily humors</a> associated water with <a href="/wiki/Phlegm" title="Phlegm">phlegm</a>, as being cold and moist. The <a href="/wiki/Water_(classical_element)" title="Water (classical element)">classical element of water</a> was also one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_elements_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Five elements (Chinese philosophy)">five elements</a> in traditional <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese philosophy</a> (along with <a href="/wiki/Earth_(classical_element)" title="Earth (classical element)">earth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fire_(classical_element)" title="Fire (classical element)">fire</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wood_(classical_element)" title="Wood (classical element)">wood</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_(classical_element)" title="Metal (classical element)">metal</a>).
</p><p>Some traditional and popular<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_philosophy" title="Asian philosophy"> Asian philosophical systems</a> take water as a role-model. <a href="/wiki/James_Legge" title="James Legge">James Legge</a>'s 1891 translation of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dao_De_Jing" title="Dao De Jing">Dao De Jing</a></i> states, "The highest excellence is like (that of) water. The excellence of water appears in its benefiting all things, and in its occupying, without striving (to the contrary), the low place which all men dislike. Hence (its way) is near to (that of) the <a href="/wiki/Tao" title="Tao">Tao</a>" and "There is nothing in the world more soft and weak than water, and yet for attacking things that are firm and strong there is nothing that can take precedence of it‚Äîfor there is nothing (so effectual) for which it can be changed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Guanzi_(text)" title="Guanzi (text)">Guanzi</a></i> in the "Shui di" Ê∞¥Âú∞ chapter further elaborates on the symbolism of water, proclaiming that "man is water" and attributing natural qualities of the people of different Chinese regions to the character of local water resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Folklore">Folklore</span></h3>
<p>"Living water" features in Germanic and Slavic <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folktales</a> as a means of bringing the dead back to life. Note the <a href="/wiki/Grimms%27_Fairy_Tales" title="Grimms' Fairy Tales">Grimm fairy-tale</a> (<a href="/wiki/The_Water_of_Life_(German_fairy_tale)" title="The Water of Life (German fairy tale)">"The Water of Life"</a>) and the Russian dichotomy of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Living_water_(folklore)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Living water (folklore) (page does not exist)">living</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">¬†[<a class="extiw" href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%B6%D0%B8%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0" title="ru:–∂–∏–≤–∞—è –≤–æ–¥–∞">ru</a>]</span> and dead water <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Dead_water_(folklore)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Dead water (folklore) (page does not exist)">dead water</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;">¬†[<a class="extiw" href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/ru:%D0%BC%D1%91%D1%80%D1%82%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%8F_%D0%B2%D0%BE%D0%B4%D0%B0" title="ru:ru:–º—ë—Ä—Ç–≤–∞—è –≤–æ–¥–∞">ru</a>]</span>). The <a href="/wiki/Fountain_of_Youth" title="Fountain of Youth">Fountain of Youth</a> represents a related concept of<a href="/wiki/Magic_(supernatural)" title="Magic (supernatural)"> magical</a> waters allegedly preventing aging.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_activism">Art and activism</span></h3>
<p>Painter and activist <a href="/wiki/Fredericka_Foster" title="Fredericka Foster">Fredericka Foster</a> curated <i>The Value of Water</i>, at the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_St._John_the_Divine" title="Cathedral of St. John the Divine">Cathedral of St. John the Divine</a> in New York City,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> which anchored a year long initiative by the Cathedral on our dependence on water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> The largest exhibition to ever appear at the Cathedral,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> it featured over forty artists, including <a href="/wiki/Jenny_Holzer" title="Jenny Holzer">Jenny Holzer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Longo" title="Robert Longo">Robert Longo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Rothko" title="Mark Rothko">Mark Rothko</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Kentridge" title="William Kentridge">William Kentridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/April_Gornik" title="April Gornik">April Gornik</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kiki_Smith" title="Kiki Smith">Kiki Smith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pat_Steir" title="Pat Steir">Pat Steir</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Kentridge" title="William Kentridge">William Kentridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alice_Dalton_Brown" title="Alice Dalton Brown">Alice Dalton Brown</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teresita_Fernandez" title="Teresita Fernandez">Teresita Fernandez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bill_Viola" title="Bill Viola">Bill Viola</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> Foster created <a class="external text" href="https://www.thinkaboutwater.com/" rel="nofollow">Think About Water</a>, an ecological collective of artists who use water as their subject or medium. Members include <a class="external text" href="https://www.basiairland.com" rel="nofollow">Basia Irland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aviva_Rahmani" title="Aviva Rahmani">Aviva Rahmani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Betsy_Damon" title="Betsy Damon">Betsy Damon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diane_Burko" title="Diane Burko">Diane Burko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leila_Daw" title="Leila Daw">Leila Daw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stacy_Levy" title="Stacy Levy">Stacy Levy</a>, Charlotte Cot√©,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Meridel_Rubenstein" title="Meridel Rubenstein">Meridel Rubenstein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stacy_Levy" title="Stacy Levy">Stacy Levy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anna_Macleod" title="Anna Macleod">Anna Macleod</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aviva_Rahmani" title="Aviva Rahmani">Aviva Rahmani</a>.
</p><p>To mark the 10th anniversary of access to water and sanitation being declared a human right by the UN, the charity WaterAid commissioned ten visual artists to show the impact of clean water on people's lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dihydrogen_monoxide_parody">Dihydrogen monoxide parody</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dihydrogen_monoxide_parody" title="Dihydrogen monoxide parody">Dihydrogen monoxide parody</a></div>
<p>Water's technically correct but rarely used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_name" title="Chemical name">chemical name</a>, dihydrogen monoxide, has been used in a series of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hoaxes" title="Hoaxes">hoaxes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pranks" title="Pranks">pranks</a> that mock <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_illiteracy" title="Scientific illiteracy">scientific illiteracy</a>. This began in 1983, when an <a href="/wiki/April_Fools%27_Day" title="April Fools' Day">April Fools' Day</a> article appeared in a newspaper in <a href="/wiki/Durand,_Michigan" title="Durand, Michigan">Durand, Michigan</a>. The false story consisted of safety concerns about the substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup>
</p>
